</ul></span></p><hr><h1 id="01-programming">01 Programming</h1><h2 id="01-programming-net">NET</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you still target netstandard?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Target <code>netstandard2.0</code> when you must support .NET Framework or older ecosystem consumers that cannot load modern <code>netX</code> assemblies.</li>
<li dir="auto">Prefer adding it as part of multi-targeting (<code>net8.0;netstandard2.0</code>) instead of making it your only target for new libraries.</li>
<li dir="auto">If all known consumers are modern .NET, target modern <code>netX</code> directly.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET Standart.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET Standart.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NET Standart</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the pragmatic default for new libraries today?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start with the modern runtime you deploy and support in production (for example <code>net8.0</code>).</li>
<li dir="auto">Add <code>netstandard2.0</code> only when compatibility requirements are explicit and validated.</li>
<li dir="auto">Reassess target frameworks periodically because old compatibility constraints often disappear over time.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET Standart.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET Standart.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NET Standart</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why are there no new .NET Standard versions after 2.1, and what does that mean for design decisions?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">.NET 5+ unified the platform, so new API evolution happens directly in modern .NET TFMs rather than via new .NET Standard contracts.</li>
<li dir="auto">.NET Standard remains a compatibility tool, not the primary innovation target.</li>
<li dir="auto">Design choice: optimize for modern .NET first, then add compatibility targets only where business requirements demand them.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET Standart.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET Standart.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NET Standart</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the three layers of the .NET platform, and why does that distinction matter?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Runtime (CLR), language (C#/F#), and framework libraries (ASP.NET Core, EF Core, extensions).<br>
It matters because most production issues cross layers: a performance problem might involve language-level allocations (C#), runtime GC behavior (CLR), and framework middleware configuration (ASP.NET Core). Understanding the boundaries helps you diagnose root causes instead of applying surface-level fixes.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NET</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between .NET Framework and modern .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">.NET Framework is Windows-only, ships with the OS, and is in maintenance mode (4.8.x patches only). Modern .NET (.NET 5+) is cross-platform, open-source, and receives active feature development.<br>
Migration matters because new capabilities (Minimal APIs, native AOT, significant performance improvements) only ship in modern .NET. Framework-era patterns (OWIN, System.Web) still appear in legacy codebases that need migration.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NET</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does .NET consistently rank high in web framework benchmarks, and does that translate to application performance?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The runtime uses tiered JIT compilation (quick startup, then optimized hot paths), the Kestrel web server is built on efficient async I/O (System.IO.Pipelines), and the framework minimizes allocations on the request path.<br>
However, benchmark performance does not automatically transfer to application code — you still need correct async patterns, minimal middleware, and efficient data access to realize it in production.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/NET.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NET</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>ASP.NET Web API</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is mapping, why is it needed, and how can it be implemented?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Mapping is the transformation of data from one shape/type to another (for example, Domain Entity -&gt; DTO -&gt; API response model).<br>
It is used to decouple layers, hide internal details, enforce API contracts, prevent over-posting, and shape data for clients.<br>
Typical implementation options:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">manual mapping (constructors, factory methods, extension methods)</li>
<li dir="auto">mapping libraries (AutoMapper, Mapster)</li>
<li dir="auto">code generation / source generators for mappings<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ASP.NET Web API</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are serialization and deserialization?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Serialization converts an in-memory object graph into a format that can be stored or transmitted (for example, JSON text or a binary payload).<br>
Deserialization is the reverse process: converting that stored/transmitted representation back into objects.<br>
Common uses: API payloads, persistence, caching, messaging.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ASP.NET Web API</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is JSON and why is it used?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">JSON (JavaScript Object Notation) is a lightweight text data format based on objects (name/value pairs) and arrays.<br>
It is widely used for data interchange, especially in HTTP APIs, because it is human-readable, language-agnostic, and easy to parse.<br>
In .NET, JSON is commonly handled with <code>System.Text.Json</code> (built-in) or Newtonsoft.Json.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ASP.NET Web API</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Where should authentication and authorization live in an ASP.NET Core API?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Put authentication and authorization in the pipeline so endpoints can assume an authenticated principal.<br>
Use middleware for auth and use endpoint metadata and policies to decide access per endpoint.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ASP.NET Web API</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When do you choose middleware over an endpoint filter?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Choose middleware for cross-cutting concerns that apply broadly (logging, auth, exception handling).<br>
Choose endpoint filters for endpoint scoped behavior and validation around Minimal APIs.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/ASP.NET Web API.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ASP.NET Web API</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can't you revoke a JWT before its expiry?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">JWTs are self-contained and the server stores no record of issued tokens. Revocation requires a server-side blacklist (typically Redis) or very short expiry (15–60 min) combined with long-lived refresh tokens stored server-side and revocable on logout.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authentication.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authentication.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Authentication</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose cookie authentication over JWT Bearer in an ASP.NET Core API?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">For browser-based web applications (Razor Pages/MVC) where the browser manages cookie transmission automatically, where session revocation (logout everywhere) is a hard requirement, and where CSRF risk is manageable via ASP.NET Core's built-in anti-forgery tokens.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authentication.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authentication.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Authentication</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does <code>ValidateIssuerSigningKey = true</code> actually enforce?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">It forces the JWT middleware to verify the token's signature against your known signing key. Without it, a token signed by a different (attacker-controlled) key could be accepted — breaking the entire authentication model.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authentication.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authentication.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Authentication</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you return 403 Forbidden vs 404 Not Found for an unauthorized resource access?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Return 403 when the resource exists but the user lacks permission, and the resource's existence is not sensitive. Return 404 consistently when leaking the resource's existence is a security risk (e.g., private financial or health records).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authorization.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authorization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Authorization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>context.Succeed()</code> and <code>context.Fail()</code> in an authorization handler?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>context.Succeed(requirement)</code> marks that requirement as satisfied. <code>context.Fail()</code> explicitly forces authorization failure regardless of other handlers' decisions — it cannot be overridden by a subsequent <code>Succeed</code>. Use <code>Fail</code> only when you have a definitive security reason to block access.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authorization.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authorization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Authorization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you implement OR logic across two authorization policies on a single endpoint?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Multiple <code>[Authorize]</code> attributes stack with AND semantics — all policies must pass. For OR logic, implement a single custom <code>IAuthorizationRequirement</code> that internally checks whether any of the conditions is met, then apply that single requirement via one policy.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authorization.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Authorization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Authorization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Does CORS protect your API from unauthorized access?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. CORS is a browser enforcement mechanism — it only affects browser-initiated JavaScript requests. Non-browser clients (curl, Postman, server-to-server calls) ignore CORS headers entirely. CORS prevents malicious websites from making cross-origin requests on behalf of a logged-in user (CSRF-style attacks), but it does not replace authentication or authorization. Always secure your API with proper auth regardless of CORS configuration.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/CORS.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/CORS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CORS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why must app.UseCors() come before authentication middleware?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Preflight OPTIONS requests do not carry authentication credentials. If authentication middleware runs first, it rejects the preflight with 401 before CORS headers are added to the response. The browser then sees a failed preflight and blocks the actual request. Placing UseCors() before UseAuthentication() ensures preflight responses include the correct CORS headers and return 200, allowing the browser to proceed with the actual authenticated request.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/CORS.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/CORS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CORS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a captive dependency and why is it dangerous?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A captive dependency occurs when a long-lived service (Singleton) captures a shorter-lived service (Scoped or Transient) at construction time. The shorter-lived service then lives as long as the Singleton, violating its intended lifetime. For DbContext, this causes a single database context to be shared across all requests, leading to data corruption and race conditions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Dependency Injection.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Dependency Injection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dependency Injection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you use a Scoped service inside a Singleton without a captive dependency?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Inject <code>IServiceScopeFactory</code> into the Singleton and call <code>scopeFactory.CreateScope()</code> at the point of use. Resolve the Scoped service from the new scope and dispose the scope when done. This ensures the Scoped service lives within a controlled scope, not captured inside the Singleton.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Dependency Injection.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Dependency Injection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dependency Injection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>GetService&lt;T&gt;</code> and <code>GetRequiredService&lt;T&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>GetService&lt;T&gt;</code> returns null if the service is not registered; <code>GetRequiredService&lt;T&gt;</code> throws <code>InvalidOperationException</code>. Use <code>GetRequiredService&lt;T&gt;</code> in production code where a missing registration is a programming error that should fail loudly at startup rather than silently return null at call time.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Dependency Injection.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Dependency Injection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dependency Injection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain when to choose middleware over an MVC action filter.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Middleware when concern is app-wide and independent of controller/action internals.</li>
<li dir="auto">Action filter when concern needs <code>ActionExecutingContext</code>, action arguments, or action result wrapping.</li>
<li dir="auto">Middleware runs earlier in pipeline and can affect all endpoints.</li>
<li dir="auto">Filters are more granular for controller/action scope.<br>
Why this matters: this is a common architecture tradeoff in API design interviews.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Filters.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Filters.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Filters</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the execution order of ASP.NET Core filter types?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Authorization -&gt; Resource -&gt; Action -&gt; Exception -&gt; Result (with before/after phases where applicable).</li>
<li dir="auto">Scope affects order: global, then controller, then action.</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>IOrderedFilter</code> can override default order.<br>
Why this matters: ordering mistakes cause hidden bugs in validation, caching, and error handling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Filters.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Filters.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Filters</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you inject services into a filter safely?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Register filter/service in DI container.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>ServiceFilter</code>/<code>TypeFilter</code> or <code>options.Filters.AddService&lt;TFilter&gt;()</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Prefer constructor injection and async interfaces.</li>
<li dir="auto">Avoid <code>RequestServices.GetService</code> inside filter bodies unless absolutely necessary.<br>
Why this matters: DI misuse in filters causes brittle code and testing pain.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Filters.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Filters.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Filters</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is middleware in ASP.NET Core?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Middleware is a component in the HTTP request pipeline. It can inspect/modify the request and response, call the next component, or short-circuit the pipeline (for example, return a cached response without calling the next middleware). Order matters — middleware registered first runs first on the way in and last on the way out.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Middlewares</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Action filter vs middleware: what is the difference?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Middleware is pipeline-level and can apply to all requests (before routing/MVC, around endpoint execution). Action filters are MVC-level and run only for MVC actions, with access to action context, model binding, and results; they are a better fit for cross-cutting concerns that are specific to controller actions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Middlewares</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How can you log execution time for all requests?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use a middleware that measures elapsed time around <code>next()</code> and logs it. An action filter works too, but only for MVC actions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Middlewares</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How can you centrally catch errors for all requests?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Add a global exception-handling middleware. In ASP.NET Core this is commonly done with <code>app.UseExceptionHandler(...)</code> (and <code>app.UseDeveloperExceptionPage()</code> in development). The handler can log the exception and return a consistent error response (for example, RFC 7807 Problem Details).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Middlewares</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the ASP.NET request processing pipeline?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A request is received by the host (for example, Kestrel) and then flows through an ordered chain of middleware. Middleware can add features (routing, authN/authZ, CORS, compression, etc.), select an endpoint, and finally execute the endpoint (MVC action, Minimal API handler, etc.). On the way back out, the middleware chain unwinds, allowing post-processing of the response.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/ASP.NET Web API/Middlewares.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Middlewares</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>CSharp</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes C# a multi-paradigm language, and why does that matter for API design?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">C# supports object-oriented (classes, interfaces, inheritance), functional (LINQ, pattern matching, immutable records, expression-bodied members), and procedural styles.<br>
This matters because you can choose the paradigm that fits the problem: OOP for domain modeling, functional for data transformation pipelines, procedural for scripts and glue code.<br>
In API design, mixing paradigms intentionally (e.g., immutable records for DTOs, interfaces for extensibility, LINQ for queries) produces cleaner, more testable code than forcing everything into one style.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/CSharp.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/CSharp.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CSharp</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does C#'s type system help prevent bugs at compile time?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Nullable reference types flag potential null dereferences before runtime.</li>
<li dir="auto">Generic constraints enforce type relationships without casting.</li>
<li dir="auto">Pattern matching with exhaustiveness checks catch missing cases in switch expressions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Strong typing prevents accidental type confusion that would be runtime errors in dynamic languages.<br>
The practical impact: many bugs that surface as runtime exceptions in Python or JavaScript are caught during compilation in C#.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/CSharp.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/CSharp.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CSharp</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h4>Concurrency and Parallelism</h4><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How is asynchrony different from multithreading?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Asynchrony is about not blocking while waiting (especially for I/O). An <code>async</code> method can release the current thread while awaiting, and continue later — potentially on the same thread.<br>
Multithreading is about executing work on multiple threads concurrently (for CPU-bound work). Async code can be single-threaded and still be asynchronous.<br>
The key cost difference: async I/O uses no thread while waiting; multithreading always occupies a thread.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Async Await</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>await</code> and <code>Task.Result</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>await</code> waits asynchronously: it does not block the current thread, it unwraps exceptions as <code>Exception</code> (not <code>AggregateException</code>), and it respects <code>SynchronizationContext</code>.<br>
<code>Task.Result</code> waits synchronously: it blocks the current thread, wraps exceptions in <code>AggregateException</code>, and can deadlock under a <code>SynchronizationContext</code>.<br>
Cost: <code>.Result</code> in a context-aware environment is a deadlock waiting to happen; <code>await</code> is the safe default.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Async Await</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use <code>ConfigureAwait(false)</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">In library code that does not need to resume on a specific context. It avoids context-capture overhead and eliminates the deadlock risk when library code is called from a context-aware environment.<br>
Do not use it in application-layer code (controllers, view models) where you need to resume on the original context to update UI or access <code>HttpContext</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Async Await</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">If async does not always use extra threads, why does it improve scalability?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because waiting time is no longer paid by tying up worker threads. Released threads can process other requests while I/O is pending. A server with 100 threads can handle thousands of concurrent I/O-bound requests if each thread is released during the wait.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Async Await</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use <code>Task.Run</code> with async code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">For CPU-bound work that you intentionally offload to a pool thread (e.g., image processing, heavy computation). Do not use it to wrap already-async I/O APIs — that wastes a thread for no benefit.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Async Await.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Async Await</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is it reasonable not to cancel immediately after the token is signaled?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When a tiny critical section must complete to keep state consistent — for example, finishing a single idempotent write or releasing resource ownership. The key is that the section is short and bounded. Never hold a lock or do I/O while ignoring a cancellation signal for an extended period.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/CancellationToken.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/CancellationToken.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CancellationToken</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is cooperative cancellation safer than <code>Thread.Abort</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Cooperative cancellation stops at known safe points under your control. <code>Thread.Abort</code> (removed in .NET 5+) could interrupt arbitrary code paths — including <code>finally</code> blocks and lock releases — leaving resources or invariants in bad state. Cooperative cancellation keeps control flow explicit and cleanup reliable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/CancellationToken.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/CancellationToken.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CancellationToken</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you propagate cancellation across a service boundary (e.g., HTTP call)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Pass the token to <code>HttpClient.GetAsync(url, cancellationToken)</code> or equivalent. The HTTP client registers a cancellation callback that aborts the underlying socket. For gRPC, pass the token to the call options. For message queues, check the token between message processing iterations.<br>
Cost: if the downstream service has already started processing, canceling the HTTP call does not cancel the server-side work — only the client-side wait.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/CancellationToken.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/CancellationToken.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CancellationToken</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between concurrency and parallelism in practice?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Concurrency is about responsiveness and non-blocking progress, often with async I/O.<br>
Parallelism is about throughput for CPU-bound workloads by using multiple cores simultaneously.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Concurrency and Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do many production outages in .NET systems look like "performance" but are actually concurrency bugs?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because thread starvation, deadlocks, lock contention, and unbounded fan-out all manifest as latency spikes and timeouts before obvious crashes.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Concurrency and Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the first decision before choosing a primitive (<code>Task</code>, <code>lock</code>, <code>Parallel</code>, <code>Channel</code>)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Classify the workload as I/O-bound vs CPU-bound and define cancellation/error boundaries. Primitive choice follows that classification.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Concurrency and Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is unbounded <code>Task.WhenAll</code> often a bad first choice when calling around 300 external APIs in one request?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because it can overload downstream dependencies, saturate connection pools, and create timeout storms. Bounded fan-out keeps latency gains while preserving system stability.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Concurrency and Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">For one requirement ("update shared state safely"), when do you choose <code>lock</code> vs <code>SemaphoreSlim</code> vs <code>Channel&lt;T&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use <code>lock</code> for short synchronous sections, <code>SemaphoreSlim</code> for async flows that need awaiting, and <code>Channel&lt;T&gt;</code> when you also need queueing, ordering, or backpressure.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Concurrency and Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Concurrency and Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is a named <code>Mutex</code> the right tool in .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When you need to coordinate access across multiple processes on the same machine (for example single-writer protection for shared file/database artifacts).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Mutex.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Mutex.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Mutex</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>Mutex</code> often a poor default for web request hot paths?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">It is OS-backed and blocking, so heavy contention can increase latency. In-process patterns (<code>lock</code>, <code>SemaphoreSlim</code>, <code>Channel&lt;T&gt;</code>) are usually more efficient.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Mutex.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Mutex.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Mutex</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does <code>AbandonedMutexException</code> signal?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A previous owner exited without releasing the mutex, which means exclusive ownership was recovered but shared state may be inconsistent and must be validated.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Mutex.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Mutex.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Mutex</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can adding more parallel workers reduce performance?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because of contention, synchronization overhead, cache misses, and context switching once you exceed useful core-level parallelism.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you decide <code>MaxDegreeOfParallelism</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Start near <code>Environment.ProcessorCount</code>, then tune with workload benchmarks and production telemetry rather than assumptions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you avoid PLINQ?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When correctness depends on strict ordering, side-effect sequencing, or when query-level debugging clarity matters more than terse syntax.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can a parallel query be slower than sequential for small inputs?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Partitioning, scheduling, and result merge overhead can dominate when per-element CPU work is too small.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Parallelism.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Parallelism</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the four Coffman conditions and which is easiest to break in practice?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Mutual exclusion, hold-and-wait, no preemption, circular wait. Circular wait is easiest to break: enforce a global lock acquisition order across all code paths. This requires discipline but no runtime overhead.<br>
The cost of consistent ordering: you must document and enforce the order, which adds coordination overhead in large codebases.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deadlocks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does calling <code>.Result</code> on a <code>Task</code> deadlock in a UI app but not in a console app?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">UI apps have a <code>SynchronizationContext</code> that marshals continuations back to the UI thread. <code>.Result</code> blocks that thread; the continuation needs it to resume — circular wait.<br>
Console apps and ASP.NET Core have no <code>SynchronizationContext</code>, so continuations resume on any pool thread and <code>.Result</code> merely blocks the calling thread without creating a cycle.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deadlocks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you diagnose a deadlock in a production .NET service?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Capture a process dump with <code>dotnet-dump collect</code> or <code>procdump</code>. Analyze with <code>dotnet-dump analyze</code> and <code>clrthreads</code>/<code>syncblk</code> commands to find threads blocked on monitors. For async deadlocks, look for threads blocked in <code>.Result</code> or <code>.Wait()</code> while holding a <code>SynchronizationContext</code>.<br>
Cost: dump capture briefly pauses the process; plan for a maintenance window or use a non-blocking snapshot tool.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deadlocks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is <code>SemaphoreSlim</code> and when do you use it instead of <code>lock</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>SemaphoreSlim</code> is an async-compatible synchronization primitive that supports <code>await</code>-able acquisition. Use it when you need mutual exclusion across <code>await</code> points — <code>lock</code> cannot span an <code>await</code>.<br>
Cost: slightly more verbose than <code>lock</code>; must manually call <code>Release()</code> in a <code>finally</code> block.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Deadlocks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deadlocks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you choose <code>SemaphoreSlim</code> over <code>lock</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Choose <code>SemaphoreSlim</code> when critical sections include <code>await</code> and you need asynchronous waiting. <code>lock</code> cannot contain <code>await</code> safely.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Semaphore.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Semaphore.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Semaphore</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is a semaphore useful for fan-out HTTP calls?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">It limits in-flight requests, protecting downstream dependencies and your own resources from overload while preserving concurrency.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Semaphore.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Semaphore.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Semaphore</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What bug pattern most often breaks semaphore-based code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Missing or unbalanced <code>Release</code> calls. The safe pattern is acquire then <code>try/finally</code> release in the same method scope.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Semaphore.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Concurrency and Parallelism/Semaphore.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Semaphore</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>Task</code> not equivalent to a thread?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>Task</code> models completion and scheduling, while threads are execution resources. Many async tasks complete I/O without holding a thread during waiting. A single thread can drive thousands of concurrent I/O tasks by processing their continuations sequentially.<br>
