<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Quick Sort.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Quick Sort.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Quick Sort</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is quick sort faster than merge sort in practice despite the same O(n log n) average?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Quick sort is in-place — it requires only O(log n) stack space versus merge sort's O(n) auxiliary array. The in-place partitioning accesses memory sequentially, giving better CPU cache utilization. Merge sort's merge step requires copying between arrays, causing more cache misses. For in-memory sorting where stability is not required, quick sort's constant factor advantage typically makes it 20–40% faster than merge sort.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Quick Sort.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Quick Sort.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Quick Sort</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does selection sort make exactly O(n) swaps, and when does that matter?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Selection sort scans the entire unsorted suffix to find the minimum, then performs exactly one swap per iteration — n−1 swaps total. This matters when writes are significantly more expensive than reads, such as flash memory with limited write cycles or EEPROM. In those cases, minimizing swaps is worth the O(n²) comparison cost. For RAM-based sorting, the swap count advantage is irrelevant — use insertion sort or Array.Sort instead.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Selection Sort.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Selection Sort.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Selection Sort</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is selection sort not stable, and how can it be made stable?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Selection sort swaps the minimum element into position, which can move an equal element past another equal element. For example, sorting [3a, 3b, 1] by value: the 1 swaps with 3a, giving [1, 3b, 3a] — the relative order of the two 3s is reversed. To make it stable, replace the swap with a shift (like insertion sort does), but that eliminates the O(n) swap advantage. In practice, if stability is needed, use merge sort.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Selection Sort.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Selection Sort.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Selection Sort</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you choose between Merge Sort and Quick Sort in production?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Merge sort gives reliable <code>O(n log n)</code> worst-case behavior and stable ordering.</li>
<li dir="auto">Quick sort is often faster in practice on in-memory arrays due to cache behavior.</li>
<li dir="auto">Quick sort has worst-case <code>O(n^2)</code> if pivot strategy is poor, so randomized or introspective variants are safer.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why it matters: this choice affects latency tail risk, memory usage, and correctness when stable ordering is required.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sorting Algorithms</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is Insertion Sort still a good choice?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">It is strong on very small arrays because constant overhead is tiny.</li>
<li dir="auto">It performs well on nearly sorted data where shifts are minimal.</li>
<li dir="auto">It is commonly used as a base case inside hybrid production sort implementations.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why it matters: knowing this avoids overengineering and explains hybrid sort internals in interviews.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sorting Algorithms</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does .NET's built-in Array.Sort use, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">.NET uses an introspective sort (IntroSort): it starts with Quick Sort for fast average performance, switches to Heap Sort when recursion depth exceeds a threshold (to guarantee O(n log n) worst case), and uses Insertion Sort for small partitions (to exploit its low overhead on nearly-sorted data).<br>
This hybrid approach demonstrates why production sort implementations combine multiple algorithms rather than using a single one.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms/Sorting Algorithms.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sorting Algorithms</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="02-computer-science-data-structures">Data Structures</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a data structure? Which ones do you know? Which of them exist in .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A data structure is a way to organize related data into a collection-like object. Examples include arrays, lists, queues, stacks, linked lists, dictionaries/hash tables, hash sets, graphs, and trees. .NET provides built-in implementations for many of these (for example <code>Array</code>, <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>Queue&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>Stack&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>LinkedList&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>Dictionary&lt;TKey, TValue&gt;</code>, <code>HashSet&lt;T&gt;</code>).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Data Structures</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you choose between List, Dictionary&lt;TKey, TValue&gt;, and HashSet?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> when you need ordered, index-based access and the primary operations are iteration or positional lookup. Use <code>Dictionary&lt;TKey, TValue&gt;</code> when you need fast lookup, insertion, and deletion by a unique key. Use <code>HashSet&lt;T&gt;</code> when you only need membership testing and set operations (union, intersection, difference) without associated values.<br>
The wrong choice shows up as O(n) scans that should be O(1) lookups.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Data Structures</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does collection choice matter more than micro-optimization?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Switching from O(n) linear search to O(1) hash lookup reduces work by orders of magnitude at scale. No amount of loop unrolling or SIMD on the O(n) path matches that.<br>
Focus on algorithmic complexity first, then optimize constant factors within the chosen structure if profiling shows it matters.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Data Structures</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you use LinkedList over List in .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Almost never in practice. <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> (backed by a contiguous array) has better cache locality, lower memory overhead per element, and faster iteration. <code>LinkedList&lt;T&gt;</code> only wins when you need frequent insertions/deletions in the middle of a very large collection and already hold a reference to the node.<br>
In most .NET code, <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> is the correct default.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Data Structures.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Data Structures</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What data structure is used behind <code>Dictionary&lt;TKey, TValue&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A hash table with bucket-based key distribution and collision handling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Dictionary.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Dictionary.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dictionary</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>Dictionary</code> usually faster than <code>List</code> for lookups?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">It computes a hash and jumps to a bucket instead of scanning each element.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Dictionary.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Dictionary.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dictionary</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does hash collision affect performance?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">More collisions increase comparisons in a bucket chain and can push operations toward O(n).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Dictionary.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Dictionary.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dictionary</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Does .NET provide a built-in <code>Graph&lt;T&gt;</code> type?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. You usually build graphs using <code>Dictionary</code>, <code>List</code>, <code>HashSet</code>, and <code>Queue</code>/<code>Stack</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Graph.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Graph.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Graph</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which collections are typically used for BFS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>Queue&lt;T&gt;</code> for frontier and <code>HashSet&lt;T&gt;</code> for visited tracking.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Graph.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Graph.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Graph</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can hash map performance degrade from O(1) to O(n)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Excessive collisions put many keys in the same bucket, so operations must compare more entries.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashMap.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashMap.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HashMap</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does a bad <code>GetHashCode</code> implementation create correctness and performance risk?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Hash maps rely on hash and equality contracts. If equal keys do not produce equal hashes, lookups can fail. If hashes are poorly distributed, bucket chains grow and performance drops.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashMap.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashMap.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HashMap</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which .NET type is the standard hash map in modern code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>Dictionary&lt;TKey, TValue&gt;</code> is the default hash map in modern .NET.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashMap.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashMap.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HashMap</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>HashSet&lt;T&gt;</code> and <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> for membership checks?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>HashSet&lt;T&gt;.Contains</code> is O(1) average; <code>List&lt;T&gt;.Contains</code> is O(n).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashSet.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashSet.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HashSet</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can <code>HashSet&lt;T&gt;.Contains</code> fail for logically equal objects?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because hash/equality contracts are broken (for example, mismatched <code>GetHashCode</code>).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashSet.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/HashSet.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HashSet</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does inserting a value into a hashtable work?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The key is hashed, mapped to a bucket, and inserted there. Collisions are handled by chain/probe resolution.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Hashtable.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Hashtable.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Hashtable</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does using a hash code instead of comparing full keys speed up lookups?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Hashing narrows search to one bucket instead of scanning all entries.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Hashtable.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Hashtable.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Hashtable</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>Dequeue</code> on a heap O(log n) instead of O(1)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Removing the root breaks heap order. The last node is moved to root and then pushed down through tree levels, which is bounded by tree height.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Heap.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Heap.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Heap</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can heap iteration look unsorted even when the structure is valid?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A heap guarantees only parent child ordering, not full in order traversal across siblings and cousins.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Heap.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Heap.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Heap</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the practical reason to use <code>PriorityQueue&lt;TElement, TPriority&gt;</code> in .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">It gives efficient dynamic priority scheduling without resorting full collections after each insert.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Heap.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Heap.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Heap</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>LinkedList&lt;T&gt;</code> often slower than <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> in real workloads despite O(1) inserts/removes?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">CPU cache locality dominates many workloads. <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> keeps data contiguous, while linked-list nodes are scattered and require pointer chasing.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/LinkedList.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/LinkedList.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LinkedList</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is <code>LinkedList&lt;T&gt;</code> the right choice in .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When your algorithm already stores <code>LinkedListNode&lt;T&gt;</code> handles and performs many localized inserts/removes around them.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/LinkedList.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/LinkedList.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LinkedList</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a common migration signal from <code>LinkedList&lt;T&gt;</code> to <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">If code frequently searches by index/value before each operation, you are paying O(n) traversal repeatedly and should usually switch to <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> or another structure.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/LinkedList.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/LinkedList.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LinkedList</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How is <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> implemented under the hood?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>List&lt;T&gt;</code> wraps an internal <code>T[]</code> buffer and tracks <code>Count</code> separately from <code>Capacity</code>.<br>
When capacity is exceeded, it allocates a bigger array and copies elements.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/List.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/List.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">List</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>Count</code> and <code>Capacity</code> in <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>Count</code> is the number of logical elements. <code>Capacity</code> is the size of the internal array.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/List.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/List.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">List</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do <code>Clear()</code> and <code>Remove()</code> affect <code>Capacity</code> in <code>List&lt;T&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">They usually change only <code>Count</code>. To shrink memory, use <code>TrimExcess()</code> or set <code>Capacity</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/List.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/List.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">List</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>Queue&lt;T&gt;</code> suitable for BFS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">BFS processes nodes in discovery order by levels. FIFO behavior naturally enforces this traversal order.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Queue.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Queue.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Queue</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you replace <code>Queue&lt;T&gt;</code> with <code>PriorityQueue&lt;TElement, TPriority&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When business correctness depends on priority rather than arrival time, such as shortest-path, scheduler, or SLA-driven dispatching.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Queue.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Queue.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Queue</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can a queue become a production reliability problem even if operations are O(1)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Complexity is not the only risk. If producers outpace consumers, memory grows and latency spikes. Throughput and backpressure design matter more than method complexity.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Queue.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Queue.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Queue</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>Span&lt;T&gt;</code> a <code>ref struct</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">It prevents the span from being boxed or moved to the heap, which protects memory safety for references to stack and unmanaged buffers.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Span.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Span.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Span</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you choose <code>Memory&lt;T&gt;</code> instead of <code>Span&lt;T&gt;</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use <code>Memory&lt;T&gt;</code> when the buffer must cross async boundaries, be stored in fields, or live longer than a single synchronous scope.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Span.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Span.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Span</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Does slicing a span allocate?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. <code>Slice</code> creates another view over the same memory region. This is why spans are useful for allocation-sensitive parsing and protocol handling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Span.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Span.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Span</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is an explicit <code>Stack&lt;T&gt;</code> better than recursion?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When graph/tree depth may be large or unbounded. An explicit stack avoids blowing the call stack and gives more control over traversal state.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Stack.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Stack.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Stack</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can <code>Stack&lt;T&gt;</code> be a poor fit for work queues?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Work queues usually require FIFO fairness. LIFO processing can starve older items and change expected behavior.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Stack.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Stack.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Stack</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the complexity of <code>Push</code> and why is it not always constant in practice?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>Push</code> is O(1) amortized. It can be O(n) during resize because elements are copied to a larger internal array.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Stack.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Stack.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Stack</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which built-in .NET collection is closest to a self-balancing tree?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>SortedSet&lt;T&gt;</code> (and <code>SortedDictionary&lt;TKey, TValue&gt;</code> for key-value scenarios).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Trees.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Trees.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Trees</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you avoid recursive tree traversal?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">On unknown/deep depth, where iterative traversal with an explicit stack is safer.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Trees.md" href="Software Engineering/02 Computer Science/Data Structures/Trees.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Trees</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="03-data-persistence">03 Data Persistence</h1><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a database?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A database is an organized collection of data stored in a structured way so it can be efficiently created, read, updated, and deleted. In practice you interact with it through a DBMS (Database Management System), which provides storage, indexing, querying, transactions, security, backups, etc.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/03 Data Persistence.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/03 Data Persistence.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">03 Data Persistence</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the main types of databases?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Common categories are: relational (SQL tables), key-value, document, wide-column, graph, time-series, and search engines. They differ in data model, query capabilities, consistency guarantees, and scaling approach.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/03 Data Persistence.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/03 Data Persistence.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">03 Data Persistence</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What isolation level should you use for a read-modify-write transaction, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use Serializable or Repeatable Read. Read Committed (the default in SQL Server and PostgreSQL) allows non-repeatable reads: a balance check and a debit in the same transaction can see different values if another transaction commits between them. For financial calculations or inventory updates, this causes incorrect results. Serializable prevents all anomalies but has the highest lock contention. Repeatable Read prevents non-repeatable reads with lower overhead. Optimistic concurrency (row version/timestamp) is a lighter alternative to Serializable for low-conflict workloads.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ACID.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ACID.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ACID</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does write-ahead logging (WAL) implement durability?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Before applying any change to data pages, the database writes the change to a sequential log file (WAL). The log write is synchronous — the transaction does not commit until the log entry is flushed to durable storage. On crash recovery, the database replays the log to restore all committed transactions and undo any uncommitted ones. WAL makes durability cheap: sequential log writes are fast; the actual data page updates can be deferred (write-behind). This is why databases can commit thousands of transactions per second despite disk I/O.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ACID.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ACID.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ACID</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes caching hard?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Invalidation and correctness. Serving data fast is easy — knowing when that data is no longer valid is the core challenge. You need a clear staleness contract per data type and safe fallbacks for when the cache is unavailable or poisoned.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you reduce cache stampede?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Add jitter to expirations so hot keys do not all expire simultaneously. Use request coalescing (singleflight pattern) so only one caller recomputes while others await the result. Consider stale-while-revalidate or background refresh to avoid blocking on recomputation entirely. <code>HybridCache</code> in .NET 9+ handles coalescing automatically.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you fix stale read-after-write behavior in Redis user-profile caching without turning off caching?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Define the correctness target first: read-your-writes for the updating user, plus a staleness budget for everyone else. On the write path, either update the cached value or delete it after the database commit succeeds. If there are multiple writers or async pipelines, publish an invalidation event and consume it in all app instances. For per-user read-your-writes, use a version token in the cache key (row version or updated_at) so the writer reads the new version immediately. Keep a TTL as a safety net for missed invalidations. Add monitoring: rate of stale reads, invalidation lag, and cache hit ratio per key type.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What changes reduce p99 spikes and database CPU saturation in TTL-only caching with synchronized five-minute expirations?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Add jitter to TTL so expirations spread over time. Add request coalescing so only one request recomputes a key and others await the same result. Consider stale-while-revalidate so you never block on recomputation for hot keys. Add a short circuit to protect the database: rate-limit recomputes, timeouts, and fallback behavior. Identify and treat hot keys separately: shorter payloads, proactive refresh, dedicated cache region.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What should be checked first when a multi-tenant cache leaks one tenant's data to another, and how is it prevented?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Assume a cache key correctness bug until proven otherwise: ensure tenant ID and auth scope are part of every key. Verify there is no shared key for different query parameters, locale, feature flags, or permissions. Ensure you do not cache error pages or partial failures that can later be served as valid results. Use strongly typed key builders and centralized key composition to prevent ad-hoc string keys. Add defense in depth: validate tenant on deserialized payload before returning and log violations.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="03-data-persistence-nosql">NoSQL</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are non-relational databases?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Non-relational databases (often grouped under the term NoSQL) store and query data without the classic relational model of normalized tables and joins. They typically favor flexible schemas and horizontal scaling, and come in several major families: key-value, document, wide-column, and graph databases.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NoSQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which NoSQL family fits a user profile API with very frequent reads by user id, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Key value or document store.<br>
Use key value if the access pattern is almost entirely by id and you do not need rich querying.<br>
Use document store if you need to read and update an aggregate document (profile + preferences) and occasionally query by a few indexed fields.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NoSQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is NoSQL a bad idea?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When your core use case needs relational constraints and multi-entity transactions, or your queries are fundamentally join heavy.<br>
In that case, add caching, read replicas, or a denormalized read model before switching storage models.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NoSQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="03-data-persistence-orms">ORMs</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does EF Core's change tracker work, and when should you disable it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">EF Core takes a snapshot of each loaded entity's property values. On <code>SaveChangesAsync()</code>, it compares current values to the snapshot and generates SQL for changed properties.</li>
<li dir="auto">Overhead: change tracking adds memory (snapshot storage) and CPU (comparison on save) per tracked entity.</li>
<li dir="auto">Disable with <code>.AsNoTracking()</code> for read-only queries (reports, API responses that don't modify data). This is the single most impactful EF Core performance optimization for read-heavy workloads.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: <code>.AsNoTracking()</code> entities cannot be modified and saved — you must re-attach them or use <code>ExecuteUpdateAsync()</code> for bulk updates.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Entity Framework</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the N+1 query problem and how do you detect it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">N+1 occurs when loading N entities and then accessing a navigation property on each, triggering N additional queries.</li>
<li dir="auto">Detection: enable EF Core query logging (<code>LogTo(Console.WriteLine)</code>) or use MiniProfiler/Application Insights to see query counts per request.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix: use <code>Include()</code> for eager loading, or split into two queries and join in memory for large result sets.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: <code>Include()</code> generates a JOIN, which can produce a large result set if the included collection is large. For collections with &gt;100 items per parent, consider <code>AsSplitQuery()</code> to use separate queries instead of a JOIN.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Entity Framework</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is an ORM?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">An ORM (Object-Relational Mapper) maps relational database tables/rows to objects and translates queries/changes in code into SQL. It helps with CRUD, tracking changes, relationships, and migrations, at the cost of an abstraction layer you must understand to avoid performance issues.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/ORMs.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/ORMs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ORMs</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="03-data-persistence-sql">SQL</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is an index and what types exist?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">An index is an auxiliary on-disk structure (usually a B+ tree) that lets the engine seek to rows without scanning the whole table.</li>
<li dir="auto">Common types: clustered, nonclustered, unique, composite, filtered, covering (nonclustered with INCLUDE), and columnstore.</li>
<li dir="auto">Clustered defines physical row order; nonclustered is a separate structure with a pointer back to the row.</li>
<li dir="auto">Columnstore stores data column-by-column for analytics; rowstore B+ tree is for OLTP.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should a column go in INCLUDE rather than the index key?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Key columns participate in B-tree navigation and are subject to the 1700-byte nonclustered key size limit (900-byte for clustered) and the 32-column key limit (SQL Server 2016+).</li>
<li dir="auto">INCLUDE columns live only at the leaf level. They bypass both limits and don't affect seek/sort behavior.</li>
<li dir="auto">Rule: SARGable columns (used in <code>WHERE</code>, <code>JOIN</code>, <code>GROUP BY</code>, <code>ORDER BY</code>) go in the key; SELECT-only columns go in INCLUDE.</li>
<li dir="auto">The goal is eliminating Key Lookup operators in execution plans. Each lookup is a round-trip to the clustered index per row.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can an index rebuild appear to fix a slow query, and how do you verify whether statistics were the real cause?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Rebuilding implicitly runs a FULLSCAN statistics update, giving the optimizer accurate cardinality estimates.</li>
<li dir="auto">Stale statistics causing a bad plan is a far more common culprit than fragmentation on SSD/cloud storage.</li>
<li dir="auto">Test first: run <code>UPDATE STATISTICS ... WITH FULLSCAN</code> without rebuilding. If that fixes the plan, fragmentation was never the issue.</li>
<li dir="auto">Rebuilding indexes purely for fragmentation on modern storage is often wasted maintenance work and causes unnecessary blocking.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is normalization and why do most systems stop at 3NF/BCNF?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Normalization eliminates redundancy by decomposing tables so each fact is stored once, preventing update anomalies. Most production OLTP systems stop at 3NF or BCNF because higher forms (4NF, 5NF) address rare anomalies (multivalued and join dependencies) at the cost of more tables and more joins. The decomposition overhead rarely pays off outside temporal or analytical databases.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Normalization Denormalization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you denormalize a table, and what risks does it introduce?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Denormalize when a read-heavy workload has expensive joins that indexes can't fix: common in reporting, analytics, or high-throughput APIs. Risks: update anomalies (copies get out of sync), data inconsistency if writes don't update all copies atomically, and increased write complexity. Always measure before denormalizing; premature denormalization adds maintenance cost for no proven benefit.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Normalization Denormalization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between 2NF and 3NF, and how would you recognize a violation of each?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
