<p dir="auto">2NF eliminates partial dependencies: every non-key attribute must depend on the entire composite primary key, not just part of it. A violation looks like a column that depends on only one column of a multi-column PK. 3NF eliminates transitive dependencies: non-key attributes must depend directly on the PK, not through another non-key attribute. A violation looks like column A depending on the PK, column B depending on A (not on the PK directly). Fixing both involves decomposing the offending columns into separate tables.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Normalization Denormalization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the three replication lag anomalies, and how do you mitigate each?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Read-your-writes</strong>: user writes then reads from a stale replica and sees their write missing. Fix: route post-write reads to the leader for a short window, or use LSN/timestamp tracking to wait for the replica to catch up.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Monotonic reads</strong>: user sees newer data on one request, then older data on the next (different replicas). Fix: sticky sessions. Pin the user to the same replica.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Consistent prefix reads</strong>: causally related writes appear out of order (a reply before the original message). Fix: route causally related writes to the same partition so ordering is preserved.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Replication.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Replication.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Replication</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose synchronous vs asynchronous replication?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Synchronous</strong>: when zero data loss is a hard requirement (financial transactions, audit logs). The leader waits for replica acknowledgment before confirming the write. Cost: every write pays the replica round-trip latency.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Asynchronous</strong>: when write latency matters more than guaranteed durability. Replicas may lag; any unconfirmed writes are lost if the leader crashes.</li>
<li dir="auto">Most production systems use async replication with one semi-synchronous replica for failover safety. SQL Server Always On supports both modes per replica. PostgreSQL <code>synchronous_standby_names</code> controls which standbys must confirm before commit.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Replication.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Replication.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Replication</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does split-brain occur and how is it prevented?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">A network partition isolates the primary from the rest of the cluster. A quorum of remaining nodes elects a new primary. When the partition heals, both nodes believe they are primary and have accepted divergent writes.</li>
<li dir="auto">Prevention: quorum-based election. A node must hold a majority of votes to become primary, so only one node can win. Fencing (STONITH) ensures the old primary is forcibly terminated before the new one starts accepting writes, eliminating the window where both are active.</li>
<li dir="auto">WSFC uses quorum witnesses; Patroni uses distributed locks in etcd/Consul. Without fencing, quorum alone is not sufficient. A slow primary that loses quorum might still be processing writes for a few seconds.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Replication.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Replication.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Replication</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between WHERE and HAVING?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>WHERE</code> filters rows before grouping and cannot reference aggregate functions. <code>HAVING</code> filters groups after <code>GROUP BY</code> and can use aggregates like <code>COUNT(*)</code> or <code>SUM(amount)</code>. If a condition doesn't depend on an aggregate, always put it in <code>WHERE</code> because it reduces the rows that enter the grouping step.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a stored procedure and how is it different from a function?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A stored procedure executes multi-step logic, can modify data, and returns result sets or output parameters. A function returns a value (scalar or table) and is designed to be called inline in a query. In SQL Server, legacy (non-inlined) scalar UDFs are evaluated row-by-row and can inhibit parallel query plans. SQL Server 2019+ may inline eligible scalar UDFs, avoiding this. Inline table-valued functions (iTVFs) are expanded like macros and don't have the parallelism problem.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a Common Table Expression (CTE) and when should you use a temp table instead?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A CTE is a named temporary result set defined with <code>WITH</code>, scoped to a single statement. It improves readability and enables recursive queries for hierarchical data. A CTE is generally treated like an inline view; don't assume it's materialized or reused across multiple references. If you reference the same CTE twice in a query, the optimizer may execute the underlying query twice. For guaranteed reuse of an expensive result, use a temp table instead.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are SQL Server transaction isolation levels?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The five isolation levels are: <code>READ UNCOMMITTED</code> (dirty reads allowed), <code>READ COMMITTED</code> (default, reads only committed data), <code>REPEATABLE READ</code> (re-reading a row returns the same data), <code>SERIALIZABLE</code> (full isolation, highest blocking), and <code>SNAPSHOT</code> (transaction-level consistent snapshot via row versioning in tempdb). Separately, Read Committed Snapshot Isolation (RCSI) is a database-level option (<code>ALTER DATABASE ... SET READ_COMMITTED_SNAPSHOT ON</code>) that makes <code>READ COMMITTED</code> use statement-level row versioning instead of shared locks, eliminating most reader-writer blocking without changing the isolation level name. Switching to <code>READ UNCOMMITTED</code> or <code>NOLOCK</code> is a common anti-pattern: you can read rolled-back, partially-written, or duplicate data.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you diagnose and optimize a slow query?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Start with the execution plan (<code>SET STATISTICS IO ON</code> or the graphical plan in SSMS). Look for table scans on large tables (missing index), key lookups (a non-clustered index was used but the engine must go back to the clustered index, or the heap via RID lookup, for extra columns; fix by including those columns in the index), and fat arrows indicating large row estimates. Check for stale statistics (<code>UPDATE STATISTICS</code>) when cardinality estimates are wildly off. For repeated slow queries, investigate parameter sniffing: a plan compiled for one parameter value may be terrible for another, fixable with <code>OPTION (RECOMPILE)</code> or <code>OPTIMIZE FOR</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you shard a database, and what should you try first?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Shard only when write throughput or storage saturates a single node. Try these first:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Vertical scaling</strong>: operationally trivial, preserves full SQL semantics.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Read replicas</strong>: offload reads but don't help writes.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Caching</strong>: reduces DB load for hot reads.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Table partitioning</strong>: splits data within one server, preserving joins and transactions.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Sharding adds permanent operational complexity and limits cross-shard queries.<br>
[!QUESTION]- Why is shard key choice critical, and what makes a good shard key?<br>
The key determines data distribution and query routing. A bad key causes hotspot shards (uneven load) or scatter queries (fan-out to all shards on every read).</p>
<p dir="auto">A good shard key has high cardinality, even distribution (avoid sequential IDs with range sharding), query alignment (appears in most WHERE clauses), and stability (changing a key requires moving the row). Common choices: user ID, tenant ID, or a composite of region + entity ID.<br>
[!QUESTION]- How does consistent hashing reduce resharding cost compared to simple modulo hashing?<br>
With <code>hash(key) % N</code>, adding one shard changes N and remaps almost every key, requiring a near-total data migration.</p>
<p dir="auto">Consistent hashing maps keys and shards onto a circular ring. Adding a shard displaces only the keys between the new shard and its predecessor, roughly 1/N of all keys. Virtual nodes (multiple ring positions per physical shard) prevent any shard from owning a disproportionately large arc.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Sharding.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Sharding.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sharding</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="04-networks">04 Networks</h1><h2 id="04-networks-architecture-ops">Architecture &amp; Ops</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is consistency hard in P2P systems?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">P2P systems have no central authority to act as the source of truth. When peers hold different versions of data and there is no coordinator to resolve conflicts, the system must rely on eventual consistency — peers eventually converge to the same state through gossip or reconciliation protocols. This is acceptable for file distribution (BitTorrent) or content-addressed storage (IPFS) where data is immutable, but it makes P2P unsuitable for systems requiring strong consistency (financial transactions, inventory).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/Peer-2-Peer.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/Peer-2-Peer.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Peer-2-Peer</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does WebRTC use P2P, and what is the role of STUN/TURN servers?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">WebRTC establishes direct peer connections between browsers for audio, video, and data. The challenge is NAT traversal: most browsers are behind NAT and don't have public IP addresses. STUN servers help peers discover their public IP/port. When direct connection fails (symmetric NAT), TURN servers relay traffic. The goal is to minimize relay usage — direct P2P connections reduce latency and server cost; TURN relay is the fallback.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/Peer-2-Peer.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/Peer-2-Peer.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Peer-2-Peer</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is split tunneling and when would you use it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Split tunneling routes only traffic destined for private resources through the VPN; internet traffic goes directly. Use it to reduce gateway bandwidth cost and latency for internet-bound traffic. Avoid it when corporate policy requires all traffic to pass through security inspection (content filtering, DLP).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">VPN</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does WireGuard have a smaller attack surface than IPsec?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">WireGuard is ~4,000 lines of code (auditable by a small team) vs IPsec's ~400,000 lines. Fewer lines means fewer potential vulnerabilities. WireGuard also uses a fixed, modern cryptographic suite (ChaCha20, Curve25519, BLAKE2) with no negotiation — removing cipher selection as an attack vector.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">VPN</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What causes DNS leaks in a VPN setup, and how do you fix them?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">DNS queries bypass the encrypted tunnel — typically because the OS sends DNS requests to the default interface rather than the VPN interface. Fix: route DNS through the tunnel by setting DNS to an internal server and ensuring DNS requests use the VPN's routing table, or by running a local DNS resolver that forces all queries through the tunnel.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">VPN</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="04-networks-protocols">Protocols</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does a DNS change "take time" to propagate?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because resolvers cache answers for the record's TTL. Old answers persist until they expire. A 24-hour TTL means up to 24 hours of propagation delay. Mitigation: lower TTL to 60–300s before planned changes, then restore it after.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">DNS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between a recursive resolver and an authoritative server?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A recursive resolver (e.g., 8.8.8.8) does the work of walking the DNS hierarchy on behalf of the client. It caches results. An authoritative server holds the actual records for a zone and answers queries about names in that zone. The resolver queries authoritative servers; clients query resolvers.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">DNS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does DNSSEC protect against cache poisoning?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">DNSSEC signs records with a private key. Resolvers verify signatures using the public key from the DNS hierarchy. A forged response without a valid signature is rejected. Cost: key rotation complexity, signing overhead, and larger DNS responses (signatures add bytes).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">DNS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What problem does HTTP/2 multiplexing solve compared to HTTP/1.1?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">HTTP/1.1 processes requests sequentially per connection — a slow request blocks all subsequent ones. Browsers work around this with 6–8 parallel connections, which is wasteful. HTTP/2 multiplexes many streams on one connection, eliminating application-layer head-of-line blocking and reducing connection overhead.<br>
Cost: a single TCP connection means TCP-layer packet loss affects all streams simultaneously.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP 2</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does HTTP/2 still have head-of-line blocking?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">HTTP/2 eliminates it at the application layer but not at the TCP layer. TCP guarantees ordered delivery — a lost packet blocks all subsequent data until retransmitted, stalling all HTTP/2 streams on that connection. HTTP/3 (QUIC over UDP) solves this with per-stream loss recovery.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP 2</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose HTTP/1.1 over HTTP/2?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the network has high packet loss (mobile, satellite) and you cannot use HTTP/3 — multiple HTTP/1.1 connections isolate packet loss better than a single HTTP/2 connection. Also when connecting to legacy servers or proxies that don't support HTTP/2.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP 2</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is creating a new HttpClient per request dangerous in .NET, and what are the two correct alternatives?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><code>HttpClient.Dispose()</code> does not immediately release sockets — the OS holds them in <code>TIME_WAIT</code> for ~4 minutes.</li>
<li dir="auto">Under load, this exhausts the ephemeral port range → <code>SocketException</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix 1: <code>IHttpClientFactory</code> with default 2-minute handler lifetime — handles both socket pooling and DNS re-resolution.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix 2: singleton <code>HttpClient</code> with <code>SocketsHttpHandler.PooledConnectionLifetime</code> set to force periodic connection recycling.</li>
<li dir="auto">Even the singleton approach has a DNS staleness trap without <code>PooledConnectionLifetime</code>.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this matters:</strong> the most common HTTP production failure in .NET; tests understanding of socket lifecycle and connection pool mechanics.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between Cache-Control no-cache and no-store?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><code>no-cache</code> means the response CAN be stored, but the cache MUST revalidate with the origin server before every use via conditional request (<code>If-None-Match</code> or <code>If-Modified-Since</code>).</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>no-store</code> means the response MUST NOT be stored at all — not in browser cache, not in CDN, not on disk.</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>no-cache</code> is appropriate for content that changes but benefits from 304 responses (saves bandwidth when content has not changed). <code>no-store</code> is for sensitive data like banking pages or PII.</li>
<li dir="auto">The naming is misleading — <code>no-cache</code> does not mean "don't cache."</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this matters:</strong> the most commonly misunderstood HTTP caching directive; wrong choice either leaks sensitive data or kills performance.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain the difference between 401 and 403 status codes.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">401 Unauthorized means the client is not authenticated — no credentials were provided, or they are invalid. The response MUST include a <code>WWW-Authenticate</code> header.</li>
<li dir="auto">403 Forbidden means the client IS authenticated but not authorized — the server knows who you are, but you lack permission for this resource.</li>
<li dir="auto">401 tells the client to retry with valid credentials. 403 tells the client that retrying with the same identity is pointless.</li>
<li dir="auto">Security consideration: some APIs return 404 instead of 403 to avoid revealing resource existence to unauthorized users.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this matters:</strong> authentication vs authorization is a fundamental security distinction; misusing these codes breaks client auth flows and retry logic.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">URI vs URL: what is the difference?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">URI (Uniform Resource Identifier) identifies a resource.<br>
URL (Uniform Resource Locator) is a type of URI that also tells you how and where to access the resource (scheme plus location).<br>
Example: <code>https://example.com/a/b?x=1#top</code> is a URL (and therefore a URI).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/Protocols.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/Protocols.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Protocols</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">A client sends <code>POST /api/orders</code>, times out, and retries. How do you prevent duplicate orders?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Require an <code>Idempotency-Key</code> header for create operations.</li>
<li dir="auto">Persist key + request hash + resulting order ID with TTL.</li>
<li dir="auto">On retry with same key and same payload, return the original result (<code>201</code> or <code>200</code>) instead of creating a new order.</li>
<li dir="auto">On same key with different payload, reject with <code>409 Conflict</code>.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> demonstrates reliability under partial failure and correct non-idempotent write handling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/REST.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/REST.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">REST</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain <code>PUT</code> vs <code>PATCH</code> semantics and retry behavior.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><code>PUT</code> replaces the target resource representation; repeated identical calls are idempotent.</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>PATCH</code> applies partial updates; idempotency depends on patch format and operation semantics.</li>
<li dir="auto">Clients can safely auto-retry idempotent requests when network failures are ambiguous.</li>
<li dir="auto">Servers should document patch media type and conflict rules (<code>409</code>/<code>422</code>).<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> tests precise HTTP semantics and failure-mode reasoning.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/REST.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/REST.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">REST</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do many teams stop at Richardson Level 2 instead of Level 3 HATEOAS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Level 2 already provides major REST benefits: resource URIs, verb semantics, status codes, cacheability.</li>
<li dir="auto">Level 3 requires hypermedia-aware clients, link relation design discipline, and tooling investment.</li>
<li dir="auto">In closed ecosystems, clients often already know workflows, reducing HATEOAS payoff.</li>
<li dir="auto">Level 3 is valuable when runtime discoverability and loose client coupling are strategic requirements.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> shows tradeoff thinking rather than dogmatic purity.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/REST.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/REST.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">REST</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is RPC's 'local call' abstraction considered leaky?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">RPC hides the network boundary, but the network introduces failures that local calls never have: calls can time out, be delivered twice (at-least-once delivery), or fail with the server having already executed the operation. A failed RPC does not mean the server didn't execute — the response may have been lost. This forces callers to implement idempotency keys, retries with backoff, and circuit breakers — concerns that don't exist for local calls. The abstraction is useful but must not be trusted blindly.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/RPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/RPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">RPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you choose gRPC over REST?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Choose gRPC for internal service-to-service communication where: (1) you need high throughput or low latency (binary Protobuf is 3-10x smaller than JSON), (2) you need bidirectional streaming, or (3) you want strongly typed contracts enforced at compile time. Choose REST for public APIs, browser clients (gRPC requires a proxy for browsers), or when human-readable payloads matter for debugging. The key constraint: gRPC requires HTTP/2 and a Protobuf toolchain; REST works with any HTTP client.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/RPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/RPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">RPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does SMTP require SPF, DKIM, and DMARC for deliverability?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">SMTP has no built-in sender authentication — anyone can claim any From address. SPF restricts which IPs can send for a domain. DKIM adds a cryptographic signature that proves the message was not tampered with. DMARC tells receiving servers what to do when SPF/DKIM fail. Without all three, email from your domain will be marked as spam or rejected by major providers.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/SMTP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/SMTP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SMTP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>System.Net.Mail.SmtpClient</code> deprecated in .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>SmtpClient</code> does not support modern authentication (OAuth2), has poor async support, and lacks IMAP/POP3. Microsoft marked it obsolete and recommends MailKit, which supports all modern protocols and authentication methods.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/SMTP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/SMTP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SMTP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does gRPC not work well with L4 load balancers, and how do you fix it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">gRPC multiplexes all calls over a single HTTP/2 TCP connection.</li>
<li dir="auto">L4 load balancers distribute at the TCP connection level — they cannot see individual HTTP/2 streams within that connection.</li>
<li dir="auto">All calls from one client land on the same backend, defeating load distribution.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix: use an L7 proxy (Envoy, Linkerd, YARP) that terminates HTTP/2 and distributes individual streams across backends. Or use client-side load balancing with service discovery.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this matters:</strong> the most common production surprise when adopting gRPC; tests understanding of HTTP/2 multiplexing at the transport layer.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">gRPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What happens if you call a gRPC service without setting a deadline?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The call has no timeout and can hang indefinitely.</li>
<li dir="auto">Resources (threads, sockets, memory) on both client and server are consumed with no bound.</li>
<li dir="auto">In a microservice chain, one hanging call can exhaust connection pools upstream, causing cascading failures across services.</li>
<li dir="auto">gRPC intentionally has no default deadline because the right value depends on the operation.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>EnableCallContextPropagation()</code> to automatically forward deadlines through a service chain.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this matters:</strong> deadlines are the single most important production gRPC configuration; missing them is the top cause of gRPC-related outages.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">gRPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is renaming a proto field safe but renumbering it is not?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Protobuf binary encoding uses the field <strong>number</strong> as the wire identifier, not the name.</li>
<li dir="auto">Renaming a field changes only the generated code accessor — the wire format is unchanged, so old and new clients interoperate seamlessly.</li>
<li dir="auto">Renumbering changes the wire identity — old clients sending the old number will have their data silently interpreted as the new field by the updated server.</li>
<li dir="auto">When removing fields, use <code>reserved</code> to prevent the number and name from being reused in future schema changes.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this matters:</strong> proto versioning is the contract management layer of gRPC; getting it wrong causes silent data corruption that is extremely hard to debug.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">gRPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="04-networks-transport-sockets">Transport &amp; Sockets</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do partial reads happen with TCP sockets?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">TCP is a byte stream protocol. The network may deliver data in smaller chunks than the sender wrote. A single <code>ReadAsync</code> call returns however many bytes are available at that moment, which may be less than the full message. Applications must loop until the expected number of bytes is received.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sockets</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>Socket</code>, <code>TcpClient</code>, and <code>TcpListener</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>Socket</code> is the low-level OS abstraction supporting TCP, UDP, and other protocols. <code>TcpClient</code> wraps a TCP socket and exposes a <code>NetworkStream</code> for stream-based I/O. <code>TcpListener</code> wraps a server-side TCP socket and provides <code>AcceptTcpClientAsync</code> for accepting connections. Use <code>TcpClient</code>/<code>TcpListener</code> for most TCP work; drop to <code>Socket</code> when you need protocol-level control.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sockets</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose UDP over TCP for a production system?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When latency matters more than delivery guarantees and the application can tolerate or recover from packet loss. Examples: real-time game state updates (stale frames are discarded anyway), DNS queries (fast retry is cheaper than TCP handshake), telemetry/metrics (occasional loss is acceptable). The application must implement its own reliability if needed.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sockets</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is UDP preferable to TCP, and what reliability mechanisms do applications add on top?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">UDP when latency matters more than reliability: real-time audio/video, gaming, DNS. A retransmitted old packet is worse than no packet. Applications add their own reliability: sequence numbers for ordering, FEC (Forward Error Correction) for loss recovery, selective retransmission for critical events. QUIC is the canonical example — it builds reliable ordered streams on UDP while avoiding TCP's head-of-line blocking.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">UDP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does UDP have no congestion control, and what are the consequences?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">UDP sends at whatever rate the application dictates. Under congestion, UDP traffic doesn't back off — it can starve TCP connections sharing the same link. Applications using UDP for bulk transfer must implement their own congestion control (QUIC does this). For small datagrams (DNS, game state), the impact is minimal because the volume is low.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">UDP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is QUIC and why is it built on UDP rather than TCP?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">QUIC (HTTP/3) implements reliable, ordered, multiplexed streams on top of UDP. It's built on UDP to avoid TCP's head-of-line blocking (a lost TCP segment blocks all streams; QUIC streams are independent), to enable faster connection establishment (0-RTT resumption), and to allow protocol evolution without OS kernel changes. TLS 1.3 is built into QUIC — there's no separate TLS handshake.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">UDP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="05-architecture">05 Architecture</h1><h2 id="05-architecture-application-architecture">Application Architecture</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the key difference between MVC and MVVM?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">In MVC, the Controller handles requests and explicitly selects which View to render — the View is passive. In MVVM, the View binds to the ViewModel's observable state — the ViewModel does not know about the View. MVVM enables automatic UI updates via data binding; MVC requires the Controller to pass data to the View on each request.<br>
Cost: MVVM requires binding infrastructure (<code>INotifyPropertyChanged</code>, <code>ICommand</code>) which adds boilerplate; MVC is simpler for stateless request/response flows.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/MVC MVVM.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/MVC MVVM.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">MVC MVVM</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is the ViewModel more testable than the Controller?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The ViewModel has no dependency on HTTP context, routing, or view rendering — it is a plain C# class. You can unit-test it by calling commands and asserting property values. The Controller depends on <code>HttpContext</code>, <code>IActionResult</code>, and the MVC pipeline, requiring more setup or integration tests.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/MVC MVVM.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/MVC MVVM.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">MVC MVVM</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the Dependency Rule and why does it matter?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">All source code dependencies must point inward — toward higher-level policies (domain). Outer layers (infrastructure, UI) depend on inner layers; inner layers never depend on outer layers. This means you can change databases, frameworks, or delivery mechanisms without touching business rules.<br>
Cost: requires defining interfaces in inner layers and wiring implementations in outer layers — more upfront structure.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Layered Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between traditional layered and Onion/Clean Architecture?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Traditional layered: UI → Business Logic → Data Access → Database. Changing the DB affects everything above. Onion/Clean: Infrastructure → Application → Domain. The Domain has zero dependencies; Infrastructure implements Domain interfaces. The key difference is dependency inversion at the data access boundary.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Layered Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose a modular monolith over microservices?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When independent deployment is not yet a hard requirement. A modular monolith with clear layer boundaries and internal APIs gives you most of the maintainability benefits of microservices without the operational complexity (distributed tracing, network failures, eventual consistency). Start here; extract services when a specific module needs independent scaling or deployment.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Layered Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you prevent version conflicts between plug-ins that depend on different versions of the same library?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use <code>AssemblyLoadContext</code> to isolate each plug-in's dependencies. Each context has its own assembly resolution scope, so plug-in A's dependency on <code>Newtonsoft.Json</code> 12.x does not conflict with plug-in B's dependency on 13.x. Cost: each context adds memory overhead and prevents type sharing across contexts — objects from one context cannot be cast to types from another.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel).md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you version extension point interfaces without breaking existing plug-ins?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Treat the extension point interface as a public API with semantic versioning. For breaking changes, introduce a new interface version (<code>IPlugin</code>, <code>IPlugin2</code>) and support both simultaneously via an adapter. Existing plug-ins implement the old interface; new plug-ins implement the new one. The core adapts old plug-ins to the new contract. Cost: adapter proliferation over time — set a deprecation timeline and remove old interfaces after a migration window.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel).md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is plug-in architecture the wrong choice?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When all features are known upfront and no third-party extensibility is needed. The loading complexity, versioning challenges, and security surface (untrusted code running in-process) are not justified for internal applications. A monolith with feature flags is simpler and safer. Plug-in architecture earns its complexity when customers or third parties need to extend the product without modifying the core.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel).md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Plug-in Architecture (MicroKernel)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="05-architecture-distributed-systems">Distributed Systems</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you design gateway aggregation endpoints for client efficiency, and what do you explicitly keep out of the gateway?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use gateway routing plus targeted aggregation endpoints for mobile-specific read models.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep gateway concerns to auth, throttling, routing, transformation, and observability.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep business rules, transactions, and domain invariants inside backend services.</li>
<li dir="auto">Add correlation IDs and tracing across fan-out calls.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> This tests whether you can design for client efficiency without turning the gateway into a distributed monolith.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/API Gateway.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/API Gateway.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">API Gateway</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose a single API gateway versus a BFF approach?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Choose a single gateway for simpler systems with similar client needs and shared release cadence.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose BFF when web/mobile/partner clients have materially different payload, auth, or latency profiles.</li>
<li dir="auto">Consider team ownership boundaries and deployment autonomy.</li>
<li dir="auto">Evaluate operational cost versus client performance and change isolation.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> This reveals whether you can apply tradeoffs, not just name patterns.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/API Gateway.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/API Gateway.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">API Gateway</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain API Gateway vs Service Mesh in one architecture and where each policy belongs.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Gateway owns north-south concerns: edge auth, TLS termination, external rate limits, API surface control.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mesh owns east-west concerns: mTLS, retries, traffic policy, and internal telemetry.</li>
<li dir="auto">They complement each other rather than compete.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> Interviewers want clear boundary thinking for platform design decisions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/API Gateway.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/API Gateway.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">API Gateway</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you guarantee read-your-writes when writes go to a strongly consistent store but reads come from an eventually consistent cache?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Route post-write reads to strong/session-consistent paths for that user.</li>
<li dir="auto">Invalidate or write-through cache on update.</li>
<li dir="auto">Include version/timestamp and reject stale follow-up writes.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep write operations idempotent.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> tests whether you can combine storage and cache guarantees without breaking read-after-write UX.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Consistency Models.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Consistency Models.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Consistency Models</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which chat features should use linearizable, causal, and eventual consistency, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Message ordering/reply chains usually need at least causal consistency.</li>
<li dir="auto">Typing indicators can be eventual.</li>
