<li dir="auto">Compliance-sensitive receipt semantics may justify stronger guarantees.</li>
<li dir="auto">Pick by user-visible correctness risk vs latency cost.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> tests whether you can map business semantics to the minimum safe consistency level.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Consistency Models.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Consistency Models.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Consistency Models</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can linearizability reduce availability during a network partition, and how do you reduce blast radius in design?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Linearizable paths need coordination/quorum that partitions can block.</li>
<li dir="auto">System must reject or delay some operations to avoid conflicting truths.</li>
<li dir="auto">Scope strong consistency to critical writes only.</li>
<li dir="auto">Serve non-critical reads with weaker models and explicit degraded-mode behavior.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> tests CAP tradeoff reasoning and your ability to design graceful degradation boundaries.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Consistency Models.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Consistency Models.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Consistency Models</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is 2PC problematic in microservices?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">2PC requires all participants to hold locks during the prepare-to-commit window. In microservices, this window spans network calls, making lock duration unpredictable.</li>
<li dir="auto">The coordinator is a single point of failure. A crash after PREPARE but before COMMIT leaves participants locked indefinitely.</li>
<li dir="auto">Most microservice infrastructure (HTTP APIs, NoSQL, cloud queues) doesn't support XA transactions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: 2PC gives strong consistency but at the cost of availability and throughput. CAP theorem: under a partition, you must choose consistency or availability — 2PC chooses consistency.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Distributed Transactions.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Distributed Transactions.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Distributed Transactions</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does the Outbox pattern guarantee at-least-once event delivery?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The event is written to an <code>OutboxMessages</code> table in the same DB transaction as the domain change. If the transaction commits, the event is durable.</li>
<li dir="auto">A background worker reads unprocessed outbox entries and publishes them to the broker, retrying until the broker acknowledges.</li>
<li dir="auto">This guarantees at-least-once delivery (the event may be published more than once if the worker crashes after publishing but before marking the entry as processed).</li>
<li dir="auto">Consumers must be idempotent to handle duplicate events.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: adds a background worker and an extra DB table. The cost is worth it for any event that must not be lost.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Distributed Transactions.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Distributed Transactions.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Distributed Transactions</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Your AI service has four instances behind an L7 gateway with passive outlier detection enabled. One instance returns HTTP 500 on about 30 percent of requests. How does the LB detect and handle this?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use active probes with failure thresholds and passive error observation from live traffic.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mark the instance unhealthy after threshold breach and remove it from new routing.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep it out until it meets consecutive-success recovery checks.</li>
<li dir="auto">If ingress is L4 or passive outlier detection is unavailable, rely on active checks plus retry and circuit-breaker policies in clients or gateways.</li>
<li dir="auto">Add retries and circuit breakers at the caller or gateway for graceful degradation.<br>
<strong>Why this question matters</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It tests production failure handling, not just basic definition knowledge.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Load Balancing.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Load Balancing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Load Balancing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is Azure Load Balancer the better choice than Azure Application Gateway for an ASP.NET Core workload?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Azure Load Balancer when L4 TCP or UDP distribution is enough and minimal overhead is preferred.</li>
<li dir="auto">Azure Application Gateway when L7 HTTP capabilities are needed, such as path routing, WAF, and rich TLS controls.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mention that Kestrel typically sits behind one of these ingress layers.<br>
<strong>Why this question matters</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It validates whether the candidate can map requirements to concrete cloud services.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Load Balancing.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Load Balancing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Load Balancing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can sticky sessions hurt autoscaling outcomes, and what architecture is safer?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Sticky sessions create uneven distribution and reduce failover quality.</li>
<li dir="auto">Scale-out does not fully rebalance existing pinned clients.</li>
<li dir="auto">Safer architecture is stateless service instances plus shared session state in Redis or a database.<br>
<strong>Why this question matters</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It checks practical judgment about reliability and elasticity tradeoffs.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Load Balancing.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Load Balancing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Load Balancing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you design webhook consumers to prevent event loss and duplicate processing?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Verify HMAC signature on every incoming webhook to reject forged payloads.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use the provider's event ID as an idempotency key; store processed IDs durably.</li>
<li dir="auto">Return <code>200</code> immediately and process asynchronously from a durable queue.</li>
<li dir="auto">Implement reconciliation: periodically call the provider's event list API to detect any missed deliveries.</li>
<li dir="auto">Make state transitions conditional (e.g., only transition an order to "paid" if it is currently "pending").<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> Tests understanding of at-least-once semantics, idempotency, and defense-in-depth for webhook reliability.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Webhooks.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Webhooks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Webhooks</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose webhooks over a shared message broker for inter-service event delivery?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Webhooks when systems cross organizational boundaries and cannot share infrastructure (SaaS integrations, third-party providers).</li>
<li dir="auto">Webhooks when the producer is an external system you do not control.</li>
<li dir="auto">Message broker when both services are internal, you need durable fan-out, back-pressure, and replay.</li>
<li dir="auto">Message broker when you need ordering guarantees per partition/key.</li>
<li dir="auto">Hybrid is common: receive external webhooks at the edge and republish to an internal broker for further processing.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> Tests whether the candidate understands the operational boundary between push-over-HTTP and broker-based messaging.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Webhooks.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Webhooks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Webhooks</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you protect a webhook endpoint against replay attacks?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Include a timestamp in the signed payload or as a signed header.</li>
<li dir="auto">Verify the timestamp is within an acceptable window (e.g., reject events older than 5 minutes).</li>
<li dir="auto">Use the delivery ID as an idempotency key to reject reprocessing even within the time window.</li>
<li dir="auto">Verify the HMAC signature covers both the payload and the timestamp so neither can be tampered with independently.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> Tests depth of security thinking beyond basic signature verification.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Webhooks.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Webhooks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Webhooks</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>Message Queues</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">You need to process order events in order per customer but handle 50K events per second. How do you design the Kafka topic?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>customer_id</code> as partition key so each customer stream stays ordered.</li>
<li dir="auto">Provision enough partitions for parallelism based on measured per-partition consumer throughput and latency targets.</li>
<li dir="auto">Scale consumer group instances up to partition count.</li>
<li dir="auto">Monitor hot partitions and lag; if one customer dominates, consider composite key strategy.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep consumers idempotent because retries and reprocessing can still happen.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this question matters:</strong> it checks whether the candidate can balance ordering guarantees and horizontal scalability.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/Kafka.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/Kafka.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Kafka</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Compare at-most-once, at-least-once, and exactly-once in Kafka. Which do you choose for payment events and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">At-most-once risks loss and is rarely valid for payments.</li>
<li dir="auto">At-least-once is common if handlers are idempotent and duplicates are acceptable.</li>
<li dir="auto">Exactly-once requires transactions and idempotent producer flow, with added latency and complexity.</li>
<li dir="auto">For payment events, choose at-least-once plus strict idempotency or exactly-once when duplicate side effects are too costly.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Why this question matters:</strong> it tests tradeoff judgment under reliability constraints.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/Kafka.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/Kafka.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Kafka</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is MSMQ still the right choice over RabbitMQ or Azure Service Bus?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">MSMQ is appropriate only when: (1) the system already uses MSMQ and migration cost is not justified, (2) the environment is on-premise Windows with no cloud connectivity, or (3) MSDTC-based distributed transactions are required. For all new systems, prefer RabbitMQ (self-hosted, cross-platform) or Azure Service Bus (cloud-managed).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/MSMQ.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/MSMQ.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">MSMQ</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is MSMQ not available in .NET 5+?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">MSMQ is a Windows-only component tied to the .NET Framework. .NET 5+ is cross-platform and does not include Windows-specific legacy APIs. The community <code>MSMQ.Messaging</code> NuGet package provides compatibility, but the recommended path is migrating to a supported broker.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/MSMQ.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Message Queues/MSMQ.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">MSMQ</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>Scalability Patterns</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What architectural prerequisites must be met before horizontal scaling works?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Service must be stateless — no in-memory session or local file state.</li>
<li dir="auto">All shared state externalized (Redis for cache/session, blob storage for files, database for persistent data).</li>
<li dir="auto">A <a data-tooltip-position="top" aria-label="Load Balancing" data-href="Load Balancing" href="Load Balancing" class="internal-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">load balancer</a> must distribute traffic across instances.</li>
<li dir="auto">Without statelessness, adding instances creates inconsistency rather than capacity.</li>
<li dir="auto">Without a load balancer, all traffic still hits one node.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It shows horizontal scaling is an architectural property, not just an ops action. Many engineers think "add more servers" is always safe.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Horizontal Scaling.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Horizontal Scaling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Horizontal Scaling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can horizontal scaling fail even with many instances?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Database becomes the bottleneck — each instance opens its own connection pool (20 instances × 100 pool size = 2,000 connections).</li>
<li dir="auto">Stateful services silently break (in-memory session on pod 1 invisible to pod 2).</li>
<li dir="auto">Uneven load distribution from sticky sessions or hot partitions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Thundering herd during scale-out when new instances aren't ready fast enough.</li>
<li dir="auto">Connection pool or thread pool saturation before CPU becomes the bottleneck.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It demonstrates awareness that scaling one tier shifts the bottleneck downstream — a classic distributed-systems trap.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Horizontal Scaling.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Horizontal Scaling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Horizontal Scaling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is <a data-href="Vertical Scaling" href="Vertical Scaling" class="internal-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Vertical Scaling</a> the better choice over horizontal?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Service is stateful and can't be refactored quickly (legacy apps, databases).</li>
<li dir="auto">Operational cost of distributed state management outweighs the capacity benefit.</li>
<li dir="auto">Need a fast fix with low risk — vertical scaling has zero architectural prerequisites.</li>
<li dir="auto">For databases, scaling up (bigger instance) is often right before introducing read replicas or sharding.</li>
<li dir="auto">No added network overhead or coordination complexity.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It shows the candidate doesn't treat horizontal scaling as universally superior — they reason about tradeoffs based on constraints.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Horizontal Scaling.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Horizontal Scaling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Horizontal Scaling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Your system handles 1,000 RPS today. Product wants 10x. How do you decide what to scale first?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start with bottleneck identification using telemetry (CPU, DB waits, p95 latency, queue depth, external dependency latency).</li>
<li dir="auto">Separate read path and write path capacity constraints.</li>
<li dir="auto">Apply highest-ROI pattern first (cache or read replicas for reads, queue for bursty async work, horizontal app scale for compute).</li>
<li dir="auto">Re-measure after each change to avoid solving a bottleneck that moved.</li>
<li dir="auto">Explicitly discuss reliability and cost impact, not just throughput.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It shows methodical scaling based on evidence, not guesswork.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Scalability Patterns.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Scalability Patterns.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Scalability Patterns</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose read replicas instead of CQRS for a scaling problem?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Choose read replicas when main pressure is read throughput on an existing relational model.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose CQRS when read/write models diverge and read projections need different shape or storage.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mention consistency behavior: replicas have lag; CQRS read models are eventually consistent by design.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mention complexity: replicas are simpler operationally than full CQRS/event projection pipelines.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It balances architecture fit, consistency, and operational cost.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Scalability Patterns.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Scalability Patterns.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Scalability Patterns</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can horizontal scaling fail even when you add many app instances?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Service may still hold state locally (sessions, in-memory cache).</li>
<li dir="auto">Shared dependencies can become the new choke point (database, third-party API, message broker).</li>
<li dir="auto">Connection pools or thread pools can saturate before CPU does.</li>
<li dir="auto">Load balancer distribution may be uneven because of sticky sessions or slow instances.</li>
<li dir="auto">Without idempotent retries and backpressure, failures amplify under load.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It demonstrates distributed-systems failure awareness beyond compute scaling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Scalability Patterns.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Scalability Patterns.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Scalability Patterns</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is vertical scaling the right first move over horizontal scaling?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">When the workload is stateful and hard to shard (e.g., a relational database).</li>
<li dir="auto">When the bottleneck is clearly resource-bound on a single node.</li>
<li dir="auto">When the operational cost of distributed coordination (consensus, partitioning, eventual consistency) exceeds the cost of a larger machine.</li>
<li dir="auto">Vertical scaling is faster to implement and avoids distributed-systems complexity.</li>
<li dir="auto">It's the right first move for most monoliths and managed databases in early growth phases.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It shows the candidate reasons about tradeoffs (complexity vs cost) rather than defaulting to "just add more servers."<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Vertical Scaling.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Vertical Scaling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Vertical Scaling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does vertical scaling have diminishing returns at high scale?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Hardware cost scales super-linearly: premium SKUs charge a multiplier for the same resource increment.</li>
<li dir="auto">Not all workloads parallelize across additional cores — Amdahl's Law bounds speedup by the serial fraction.</li>
<li dir="auto">A process with 20% serial code can never exceed 5x speedup regardless of core count.</li>
<li dir="auto">Memory bandwidth and cache coherency become bottlenecks as core counts grow.</li>
<li dir="auto">The cost-per-unit-of-capacity curve inflects sharply at high tiers.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It demonstrates understanding of hardware economics and parallelism limits, not just "bigger is better."<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Vertical Scaling.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Vertical Scaling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Vertical Scaling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What's the failure mode of relying solely on vertical scaling for availability?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">A single large node is a single point of failure — any hardware fault, OS crash, or maintenance window causes full outage.</li>
<li dir="auto">Vertical scaling increases capacity but does nothing for availability.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mitigation: pair with redundancy (active-passive failover, read replicas, multi-AZ deployment).</li>
<li dir="auto">Azure SQL Business Critical tier includes a built-in secondary replica for reads and failover.</li>
<li dir="auto">This is distinct from <a data-href="Horizontal Scaling" href="Horizontal Scaling" class="internal-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Horizontal Scaling</a>, which inherently distributes failure across nodes.<br>
<strong>Why this is strong:</strong> It shows the candidate distinguishes capacity from availability — a common interview blind spot.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Vertical Scaling.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/Scalability Patterns/Vertical Scaling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Vertical Scaling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="05-architecture-patterns">Patterns</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does CQS make code easier to reason about?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When a method returns a value, you know it has no side effects — you can call it freely, cache its result, or call it multiple times without changing state. When a method returns void, you know it changes state but produces no data you depend on. This separation eliminates the need to read the implementation to understand whether a call is safe to repeat or reorder. It also makes testing easier: queries can be tested without checking state changes; commands can be tested without checking return values.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/CQS.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/CQS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CQS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is it pragmatic to violate CQS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Three common justified exceptions: (1) Stack.Pop() — splitting into Peek() + Remove() introduces a race condition in concurrent code. (2) Repository.Add() returning the generated ID — the ID is produced by the database; returning it avoids an extra round-trip. (3) Async I/O methods — Task methods that perform I/O and return a result are idiomatic in .NET even when they have side effects. The principle is a guideline: apply it where it improves clarity, relax it where strict adherence creates awkward APIs.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/CQS.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/CQS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CQS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Your singleton cache service must use a scoped <code>DbContext</code> to warm data on startup. How do you solve this without captive dependency?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong> inject <code>IServiceScopeFactory</code>, create a scope inside startup logic, resolve <code>DbContext</code> from that scope, perform work, dispose scope.<br>
<strong>Why:</strong> validates practical lifetime-boundary reasoning, not just memorized definitions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Dependency Injection.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Dependency Injection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dependency Injection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain <code>Transient</code>, <code>Scoped</code>, and <code>Singleton</code> lifetimes with one safe production example each.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong> transient for lightweight stateless services, scoped for request-bound consistency (<code>DbContext</code>), singleton for thread-safe shared services (cache/config/factory).<br>
<strong>Why:</strong> checks whether candidate maps lifetime to runtime behavior.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Dependency Injection.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Dependency Injection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dependency Injection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is Service Locator an anti-pattern in business logic, and when is it acceptable?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong> it hides dependencies and hurts testability; acceptable in factories, middleware activation, and explicit scope-managed infrastructure.<br>
<strong>Why:</strong> tests architecture judgment and boundary discipline.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Dependency Injection.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Dependency Injection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Dependency Injection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does EF Core's DbContext already implement the Unit of Work pattern?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><code>DbContext</code> tracks all changes to loaded entities in its change tracker.</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>SaveChangesAsync()</code> wraps all pending inserts, updates, and deletes in a single database transaction.</li>
<li dir="auto">This means multiple repository operations within one request share the same <code>DbContext</code> instance and commit atomically — exactly what Unit of Work provides.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: this only works if all repositories share the same <code>DbContext</code> instance (Scoped lifetime in ASP.NET Core DI). If you accidentally register <code>DbContext</code> as Singleton or Transient, the Unit of Work semantics break.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Repository &amp; UoW.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Repository &amp; UoW.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Repository &amp; UoW</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is a generic IRepository an anti-pattern?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">A generic repository exposes the same interface for every entity, including operations that don't make sense for some aggregates (e.g., <code>GetAll()</code> on an <code>Order</code> aggregate with millions of rows).</li>
<li dir="auto">It encourages callers to treat all entities the same, bypassing aggregate-specific access patterns and invariants.</li>
<li dir="auto">It often leaks <code>IQueryable&lt;T&gt;</code>, coupling callers to EF Core.</li>
<li dir="auto">Better: aggregate-specific interfaces with domain-meaningful methods. Use a generic base class for the implementation, but expose a specific interface.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Repository &amp; UoW.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Repository &amp; UoW.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Repository &amp; UoW</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>Architectural Patterns</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is CQRS worth the operational complexity, and when is it an anti-pattern?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong> CQRS is justified when read/write workloads differ materially (high read ratio, complex query shapes, independent scaling needs) and the domain has meaningful invariants. It is usually an anti-pattern for simple CRUD with low scale pressure, because projection pipelines, eventual consistency handling, and dual-model maintenance add avoidable complexity.<br>
[!QUESTION]- Your dashboard queries are slow because the normalized write model needs 5 JOINs. How does CQRS help, and what is the cost?<br>
<strong>Expected answer:</strong> CQRS allows a denormalized read model built for that dashboard (for example, a pre-joined projection table), which removes expensive runtime joins and improves latency. The cost is projection infrastructure, eventual consistency, duplicate data, and extra operational work (retries, idempotency, lag monitoring). The key interview point is to justify CQRS only if latency/scaling gains outweigh this complexity.<br>
[!QUESTION]- Does adopting CQRS mean you must adopt Event Sourcing?<br>
<strong>Expected answer:</strong> No. CQRS is a responsibility split between command and query paths; Event Sourcing is a persistence strategy where events are the source of truth. They fit well together because events project naturally to read models, but CQRS can run on regular state-based persistence (for example, EF Core tables) with or without event publication.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Architectural Patterns/CQRS.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Architectural Patterns/CQRS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CQRS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between an Entity and a Value Object?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Entity: has a unique identity that persists over time. Two entities with the same data are different if their IDs differ. Example: <code>Order</code> with <code>OrderId</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Value Object: defined entirely by its attributes, no identity. Two Value Objects with the same data are equal. Immutable. Example: <code>Money(10, "USD")</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Rule of thumb: if you care about <em>which</em> instance it is, it's an Entity. If you only care about <em>what</em> it contains, it's a Value Object.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: Value Objects are simpler to reason about (immutable, no identity tracking) but require copying on mutation. Use them for concepts like Money, Address, DateRange, Coordinates.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Architectural Patterns/Domain-Driven Design.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Architectural Patterns/Domain-Driven Design.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Domain-Driven Design</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why should external code only interact with an Aggregate through its root?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The Aggregate Root enforces all invariants for the cluster. If external code modifies a <code>LineItem</code> directly, it bypasses the <code>Order</code>'s consistency checks.</li>
<li dir="auto">Example: adding a line item after an order is confirmed should be rejected. If <code>LineItem</code> is modified directly, the <code>Order</code> never gets a chance to enforce this rule.</li>
<li dir="auto">Practical implication: repositories load and save entire Aggregates, not individual child entities. EF Core's change tracking makes this natural.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: loading the full Aggregate for every operation can be expensive if the Aggregate is large. This is a signal that the Aggregate boundary is too wide.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Architectural Patterns/Domain-Driven Design.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Architectural Patterns/Domain-Driven Design.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Domain-Driven Design</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>Design Patterns</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are design patterns and why do we need them?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Design patterns are proven, repeatable approaches to common design problems (not copy-paste code). They communicate intent, reduce accidental complexity, and improve maintainability through loose coupling.<br>
Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Named reusable design templates.</li>
<li dir="auto">Shared team vocabulary.</li>
<li dir="auto">Better extensibility and testability.<br>
Why this matters: interviewers check whether you think in reusable design decisions instead of ad-hoc classes.<br>
[!QUESTION]- What categories of patterns exist?<br>
For GoF patterns, the classic categories are:</li>
<li dir="auto">Creational: object creation (how instances are constructed)</li>
<li dir="auto">Structural: object composition (how classes/objects are arranged)</li>
<li dir="auto">Behavioral: object interaction (how responsibilities and communication are organized)<br>
Expected answer:</li>
<li dir="auto">Creational, Structural, Behavioral.</li>
<li dir="auto">Classify by where change pressure appears.<br>
Why this matters: category mapping shows fast architectural reasoning and helps justify pattern choice under time pressure.<br>
[!QUESTION]- What is an anti-pattern?<br>
An anti-pattern is a recurring solution that appears useful but repeatedly causes high coupling, low testability, or operational problems.<br>
Expected answer:</li>
<li dir="auto">Recognize recurring harmful design choices.</li>
<li dir="auto">Explain why they fail over time.</li>
<li dir="auto">Propose a safer alternative.<br>
Why this matters: strong candidates avoid cargo-cult patterns and can explain failure mechanisms, not only definitions.<br>
[!QUESTION]- Name a few patterns from each category and the basic idea behind them.<br>
Creational:</li>
<li dir="auto">Factory Method: delegate creation to creators/factories to decouple callers from concrete types.</li>
<li dir="auto">Builder: construct complex objects step-by-step.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Structural:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Adapter: make incompatible interfaces work together.</li>
<li dir="auto">Decorator: add behavior by wrapping instead of subclassing.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Behavioral:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Strategy: swap algorithms behind a common interface.</li>
<li dir="auto">Observer: publish/subscribe notifications.<br>
Expected answer:</li>
<li dir="auto">Give one concrete use-case per chosen pattern.<br>
Why this matters: interviewers score practical mapping from pattern names to real design problems.<br>
[!QUESTION]- You need to add a new robot type to an existing system without modifying existing code. Which patterns enable this?<br>
Combine Factory Method (instantiate new robot implementation), Strategy (inject new movement/task behavior), and Template Method (reuse stable execution skeleton).<br>
Expected answer:</li>
<li dir="auto">Factory Method for creation extension.</li>
<li dir="auto">Strategy for algorithm variation.</li>
<li dir="auto">Template Method for stable lifecycle.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: extra classes and abstraction overhead.<br>
Why this matters: this question tests OCP-driven design and whether you can combine patterns coherently.<br>
[!QUESTION]- How do you choose between Strategy and Template Method in robot behavior design?<br>
Prefer Strategy when behavior slices vary independently and should be swapped by composition. Prefer Template Method when algorithm order is fixed and only selected steps should vary.<br>
Expected answer:</li>
<li dir="auto">Strategy: composition and runtime swap, with more object wiring.</li>
<li dir="auto">Template Method: inheritance with a fixed skeleton and tighter base-class coupling.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: flexibility vs orchestration simplicity.<br>
Why this matters: this is a common class-design tradeoff where poor choice leads to either rigid inheritance or excessive object churn.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Design Patterns/Design Patterns.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Design Patterns/Design Patterns.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Design Patterns</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the Information Expert principle and why does it reduce coupling?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Information Expert assigns responsibility to the class that has the information needed to fulfill it. This keeps related data and behavior together, reducing the need for one class to reach into another to get data. The result is lower coupling and higher cohesion — each class does what it knows.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Design Patterns/GRASP.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Design Patterns/GRASP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">GRASP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does GRASP differ from SOLID?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">GRASP focuses on responsibility assignment — which class should own a behavior or data. SOLID focuses on design principles for maintainability (single responsibility, open/closed, etc.). They are complementary: GRASP guides initial design decisions; SOLID guides refactoring and long-term maintainability.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Design Patterns/GRASP.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Design Patterns/GRASP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">GRASP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>Resilience Patterns</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="" data-callout="question" class="callout node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Your AI service wraps OpenAI APIs with per-tenant limits and runs on 4 instances. How do you enforce limits accurately, and which algorithm do you choose?</div></div><div class="callout-content">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use distributed shared state, usually Redis, because per-instance memory breaks global accuracy.</li>
<li dir="auto">Partition by tenant ID so quotas align with billing and fairness.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose token bucket when tenants need controlled burst capacity with stable average throughput.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use atomic operations (Lua or transaction pattern) for refill and consume to avoid race conditions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Return <code>429</code> with <code>Retry-After</code> and remaining quota headers to support client backoff.<br>
<strong>Why this question matters</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It tests algorithm choice plus distributed systems correctness, not just definition recall.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Resilience Patterns/Rate Limiting.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Resilience Patterns/Rate Limiting.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Rate Limiting</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="" data-callout="question" class="callout node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you prefer sliding window counter over fixed window in a public API?</div></div><div class="callout-content">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Prefer sliding window counter when edge fairness matters and fixed window boundary bursts are unacceptable.</li>
<li dir="auto">It gives near-rolling behavior with lower memory than sliding log.</li>
<li dir="auto">Accept approximation error in exchange for better operational cost.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep fixed window only where simplicity dominates and traffic patterns are predictable.<br>
<strong>Why this question matters</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It checks whether the candidate can justify tradeoffs under realistic constraints.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Resilience Patterns/Rate Limiting.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Resilience Patterns/Rate Limiting.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Rate Limiting</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="" data-callout="question" class="callout node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What failure mode should you choose if Redis-based rate limiting is unavailable: fail-open or fail-closed?</div></div><div class="callout-content">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Decide by endpoint risk profile, not globally.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fail-open for low-risk endpoints when availability is the top priority.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fail-closed for sensitive operations where abuse or cost explosion is unacceptable.</li>
<li dir="auto">Document and test the behavior with chaos drills.<br>
<strong>Why this question matters</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It tests operational judgment and explicit risk tradeoff reasoning.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Resilience Patterns/Rate Limiting.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Patterns/Resilience Patterns/Rate Limiting.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Rate Limiting</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="05-architecture-system-architecture">System Architecture</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Order service publishes <code>OrderPlaced</code>. Payment and Inventory both consume it. Payment fails. How do you handle compensation without tight coupling?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Prefer saga/process manager or choreography with explicit failure events (<code>PaymentFailed</code>).</li>
<li dir="auto">Avoid direct service-to-service rollback calls as the primary mechanism.</li>
<li dir="auto">Emit compensating commands/events (<code>ReleaseInventory</code>, <code>CancelOrder</code>) based on workflow state.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep operations idempotent because retries and duplicates are expected.</li>
<li dir="auto">Track workflow with correlation ID for observability and replay.<br>
<strong>Why this is asked</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It tests whether the candidate understands eventual consistency and compensation in distributed systems.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Event-Driven Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Event-Driven Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Event-Driven Architecture</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose orchestration over choreography in an event-driven workflow?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Choose orchestration when the process is long-running, has strict step ordering, and requires explicit compensation/state visibility.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choreography is better when services can react independently and team autonomy is a top priority.</li>
<li dir="auto">Orchestration centralizes control (easier reasoning) but adds coordinator complexity and potential bottleneck risk.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choreography reduces central coupling but raises tracing and governance complexity as fan-out grows.<br>
<strong>Why this is asked</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It tests architectural judgment under operational constraints, not just pattern definitions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Event-Driven Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Event-Driven Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Event-Driven Architecture</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you evolve integration event contracts without breaking consumers?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use additive changes first; avoid removing/renaming fields abruptly.</li>
<li dir="auto">Version contracts (<code>v1</code>, <code>v2</code>) and support dual publishing during migrations.</li>
<li dir="auto">Add consumer-driven contract tests in CI.</li>
<li dir="auto">Monitor deserialization failures and rollout with staged deployments.<br>
<strong>Why this is asked</strong></li>
<li dir="auto">It tests real-world ownership of compatibility, release safety, and producer-consumer decoupling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Event-Driven Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Event-Driven Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Event-Driven Architecture</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can microservices lead to distributed data consistency problems, and how do you address them?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Each service owns its data, so cross-service business actions cannot rely on one ACID transaction.</li>
<li dir="auto">A later step can fail after an earlier local commit, producing partial state.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use sagas with compensating actions across local transactions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use outbox/inbox and idempotent handlers to survive retries and duplicates.</li>
<li dir="auto">Accept eventual consistency and make process state observable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Microservices.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Microservices.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Microservices</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Order and Inventory are separate microservices. A customer places an order. How do you ensure inventory is reserved without a distributed transaction?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Persist order as <code>Pending</code> and publish <code>OrderPlaced</code> via outbox in the same local transaction.</li>
<li dir="auto">Inventory consumes <code>OrderPlaced</code> and emits <code>InventoryReserved</code> or <code>InventoryRejected</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Order transitions to <code>Confirmed</code> or <code>Rejected</code> based on the inventory event.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep consumers idempotent by tracking processed message IDs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Add timeout compensation if inventory does not respond within SLA.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Microservices.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Microservices.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Microservices</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you decide between monolith, modular monolith, and microservices for a new product?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Decide from team size, release pressure, domain volatility, and ops maturity.</li>
<li dir="auto">One team in discovery phase usually benefits most from monolith speed.</li>
<li dir="auto">Clear domains with limited platform capacity often fit modular monolith.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose microservices when independent deploy/scale constraints are proven.</li>
<li dir="auto">Re-evaluate architecture periodically as constraints change.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Microservices.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Microservices.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Microservices</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a modular monolith and when is it better than microservices?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A modular monolith enforces explicit module boundaries (clear interfaces, no internal coupling) within a single deployment. It gives you maintainability and team autonomy without distributed systems complexity. It is better than microservices when independent deployment is not yet a hard requirement — which is most early-stage products.<br>
Cost: requires discipline to maintain module boundaries; without enforcement, it degrades into a big ball of mud.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Monolith Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Monolith Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monolith Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When do microservices become justified over a monolith?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When independent deployment is a hard requirement and the team can support the operational complexity (distributed tracing, network failures, eventual consistency, service mesh). The signal is usually: teams are blocked by each other's deployments, or a specific component needs independent scaling that the monolith cannot provide.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Monolith Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Monolith Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monolith Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you mitigate cold start latency in serverless functions?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Cold starts occur when the provider scales from zero — the runtime must boot, load the function, and initialize dependencies (500ms–3s for .NET).
