Mitigations: (1) Premium Plan (Azure) or Provisioned Concurrency (AWS) keeps instances warm at a fixed cost. (2) Native AOT compilation reduces .NET startup time by eliminating JIT. (3) Minimize dependencies loaded at startup — lazy-initialize anything not needed on every invocation. Accept cold starts for background jobs where latency doesn't matter; eliminate them for latency-sensitive APIs.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Serverless Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Serverless Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Serverless Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you avoid vendor lock-in with serverless functions?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Isolate business logic from the function host. The function handler should be a thin adapter that reads the trigger event, calls a provider-agnostic service, and returns a response. The core logic lives in a class library with no provider-specific dependencies. This makes the business logic portable even if the host (Azure Functions, AWS Lambda) is not. Cost: requires discipline to keep the adapter thin; teams often let provider-specific bindings leak into business logic.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Serverless Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Serverless Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Serverless Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you model cost for a serverless workload vs a container-based one?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Serverless: cost = (invocations × duration × memory) + egress. Scales to zero when idle — no cost for unused capacity. Container: cost = (instances × uptime) regardless of traffic. Serverless wins for bursty or infrequent workloads; containers win for steady high-throughput traffic where the per-invocation overhead adds up. The crossover point is roughly when serverless cost exceeds the minimum container cost at sustained load.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Serverless Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Serverless Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Serverless Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the key architectural difference between SOA and microservices?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">SOA uses an Enterprise Service Bus (ESB) as a central integration hub — services communicate through the bus, which handles routing, transformation, and orchestration. Microservices use 'smart endpoints, dumb pipes': services contain the logic and communicate directly via HTTP/REST, gRPC, or lightweight message brokers. The ESB becomes a bottleneck and single point of failure in SOA; microservices distribute that responsibility into the services themselves.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Service-Oriented Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Service-Oriented Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Service-Oriented Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is SOA still the right choice over microservices?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">SOA remains appropriate for enterprise integration scenarios: connecting heterogeneous legacy systems (SAP, Salesforce, custom ERP) via a shared integration layer, shared services used by multiple business units where microservice decomposition overhead isn't justified, and regulated industries requiring centralized governance and audit trails. Microservices are better for greenfield cloud-native systems where teams can own independent services end-to-end.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Service-Oriented Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/System Architecture/Service-Oriented Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Service-Oriented Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="06-development-practices">06 Development Practices</h1><h2 id="06-development-practices-paradigms">Paradigms</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does event-driven architecture differ from event sourcing?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Event-driven architecture is a communication style: components publish events and others react asynchronously. Event sourcing is a persistence pattern: the state of an entity is derived by replaying its event history rather than storing current state. You can use event-driven communication without event sourcing (most systems do). Event sourcing requires event-driven communication but adds the constraint that events are the source of truth for state.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Event-driven.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Event-driven.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Event-driven</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you guarantee ordering of events in a distributed event-driven system?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Most message brokers guarantee ordering only within a partition or queue. Kafka guarantees ordering within a partition (use a consistent partition key, e.g., order ID). Azure Service Bus sessions guarantee ordering within a session. Cross-partition ordering is not guaranteed — design consumers to be idempotent and handle out-of-order delivery. If strict global ordering is required, use a single partition (which limits throughput) or a sequencer service.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Event-driven.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Event-driven.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Event-driven</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why must event consumers be idempotent in an at-least-once delivery system?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Most message brokers guarantee at-least-once delivery: a message may be delivered more than once if the consumer crashes after processing but before acknowledging. Without idempotency, duplicate delivery causes double-charging, double-reserving, or duplicate records. Mitigation: track processed event IDs in a <code>ProcessedEvents</code> table and skip duplicates, or design operations to be naturally idempotent (SET stock = X instead of stock -= Y).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Event-driven.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Event-driven.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Event-driven</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes a function "pure" and why does purity matter for testing?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">A pure function has no side effects and is referentially transparent: same inputs always produce the same output.</li>
<li dir="auto">No side effects means no I/O, no global state reads/writes, no exceptions from external dependencies.</li>
<li dir="auto">Testing cost: pure functions need zero mocks — just call with inputs and assert outputs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Parallelism: pure functions are safe to run concurrently without locks.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: real systems need side effects (DB writes, HTTP calls). The FP discipline is to push side effects to the edges and keep the core logic pure.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Functional Programming.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Functional Programming.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Functional Programming</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does immutability prevent bugs in concurrent code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Shared mutable state is the root cause of race conditions: two threads read-modify-write the same object.</li>
<li dir="auto">Immutable objects can be shared freely across threads — no lock needed because no mutation is possible.</li>
<li dir="auto">In C#: <code>record</code> types with <code>init</code>-only properties, <code>ImmutableDictionary&lt;K,V&gt;</code>, <code>string</code> (already immutable).</li>
<li dir="auto">Cost: every "mutation" allocates a new object. Acceptable for domain events and DTOs; expensive for high-frequency data structures.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: immutability shifts cost from runtime synchronization to GC pressure. Profile before applying everywhere.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Functional Programming.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Functional Programming.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Functional Programming</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose LINQ over a manual loop in production code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">LINQ: when the transformation is a pipeline of filter/map/reduce steps and readability matters more than micro-performance.</li>
<li dir="auto">Manual loop: when you need early exit with complex state, when profiling shows LINQ overhead is significant, or when you need to avoid multiple enumerations.</li>
<li dir="auto">Key risk with LINQ: deferred execution — materializing with <code>.ToList()</code> at the right point is non-obvious and a common source of double-query bugs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: LINQ is more declarative and composable; loops are more explicit about control flow and allocation. In hot paths (&gt;10k iterations/sec), benchmark both.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Functional Programming.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Functional Programming.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Functional Programming</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does <code>WebApplicationFactory</code> test that unit tests cannot?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">DI registration: if a service is missing from the container, the integration test fails at startup.</li>
<li dir="auto">Middleware ordering: authentication, authorization, exception handling, and routing all run in the real pipeline.</li>
<li dir="auto">Serialization contracts: JSON serialization settings (camelCase, nullable handling, custom converters) are applied.</li>
<li dir="auto">Controller binding: model validation, route constraints, and action filters run as they would in production.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: <code>WebApplicationFactory</code> tests are slower than unit tests (seconds vs milliseconds). Run them in a separate test project so they don't slow down the unit test feedback loop.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Integration Testing.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Integration Testing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Integration Testing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use Testcontainers instead of EF In-Memory?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">When your queries use SQL features that EF In-Memory doesn't support: raw SQL, stored procedures, database constraints, <code>RETURNING</code> clauses, CTEs.</li>
<li dir="auto">When you need to test database migrations (EF In-Memory doesn't run migrations).</li>
<li dir="auto">When a bug was caused by SQL behavior that the in-memory provider masked.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: Testcontainers requires Docker in CI and adds 5-15 seconds of container startup per test class. The cost is worth it when in-memory tests give false confidence.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Integration Testing.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Integration Testing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Integration Testing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you prefer composition over inheritance?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Prefer composition when the relationship is 'has-a' rather than 'is-a', when you need to combine behaviors from multiple sources (C# has no multiple inheritance), or when the base class is not designed for extension (sealed or has complex invariants). Inheritance creates tight coupling: a change to the base class can break all derived classes. Composition lets you swap implementations at runtime and test components in isolation. Rule of thumb: if you find yourself overriding methods to change or suppress base behavior, composition is the better fit.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/OOP.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/OOP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OOP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the Anemic Domain Model and why is it considered an anti-pattern?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">An Anemic Domain Model has objects with only getters/setters and no behavior — all logic lives in service classes. It violates encapsulation: the service must reach into the object to get data, then compute and set results back. This is procedural programming with OOP syntax. The cost: invariants are enforced in service code (scattered, easy to miss), objects can be put into invalid states, and the domain model provides no design guidance. Fix: move behavior that depends on an object's data into the object itself (Information Expert principle).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/OOP.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/OOP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OOP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does polymorphism reduce conditional complexity?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Without polymorphism, behavior that varies by type requires if/switch chains: 'if type == PDF, do X; if type == CSV, do Y.' Each new type requires modifying existing code (Open/Closed violation). With polymorphism, each type implements a shared interface and handles its own behavior. Adding a new type means adding a new class, not modifying existing code. The caller iterates over <code>IEnumerable&lt;IReportGenerator&gt;</code> and calls <code>Generate()</code> — the runtime dispatches to the correct implementation.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/OOP.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/OOP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OOP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does TDD improve design, not just test coverage?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Writing the test first forces you to define the public interface before the implementation exists.</li>
<li dir="auto">If the test is hard to write (too many constructor arguments, complex setup), that's a design signal: the class has too many responsibilities or too many dependencies.</li>
<li dir="auto">TDD naturally produces smaller classes with single responsibilities because each test targets one behavior.</li>
<li dir="auto">Test-after doesn't provide this signal — the implementation already exists and the test is shaped around it.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: TDD's design benefit requires discipline. Skipping the Red step (writing a test that already passes) eliminates the feedback loop.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Test-Driven Development.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Test-Driven Development.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Test-Driven Development</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is TDD not worth the overhead?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Exploratory/spike code: you don't know the right design yet. Write the spike without tests, learn from it, then delete it and rebuild with TDD.</li>
<li dir="auto">UI rendering logic: visual behavior is hard to unit-test meaningfully; use snapshot tests or manual QA instead.</li>
<li dir="auto">Trivial CRUD with no branching: a single-line property setter doesn't need a test-first cycle.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: TDD overhead is ~20-30% more time upfront. The payback is faster debugging and safer refactoring over the life of the codebase. For short-lived code, the payback never arrives.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Test-Driven Development.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Test-Driven Development.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Test-Driven Development</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you handle external dependencies (DB, HTTP) in TDD?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Inject dependencies as interfaces. In tests, provide a fake or in-memory implementation.</li>
<li dir="auto">For repositories: use an in-memory implementation (e.g., <code>Dictionary&lt;Guid, Order&gt;</code>) rather than mocking every method.</li>
<li dir="auto">For HTTP clients: use <code>HttpMessageHandler</code> fakes or <code>WireMock.Net</code> for realistic HTTP stubs.</li>
<li dir="auto">For time: inject <code>TimeProvider</code> (built into .NET 8+) so tests can control "now" without <code>DateTime.UtcNow</code> coupling.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: fakes require maintenance. If the real implementation changes behavior, the fake may diverge. Contract tests (testing the fake against the real implementation) catch this.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Test-Driven Development.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Test-Driven Development.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Test-Driven Development</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between a stub and a mock?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">A stub provides canned return values so the test can proceed — it answers questions ("what orders does customer X have?").</li>
<li dir="auto">A mock verifies interactions — it records calls and lets you assert that a specific method was called with specific arguments.</li>
<li dir="auto">Practical rule: stub data sources (repositories, config); mock side-effect sinks (email, SMS, audit log, event bus).</li>
<li dir="auto">Over-mocking (mocking everything including internal collaborators) produces tests that break on every refactor without catching real bugs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: mocks couple tests to implementation details. Prefer fakes (working in-memory implementations) when the dependency has non-trivial behavior.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Unit Testing.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Unit Testing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Unit Testing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you test code that depends on the current time?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Inject <code>TimeProvider</code> (built into .NET 8+) instead of calling <code>DateTime.UtcNow</code> directly.</li>
<li dir="auto">In tests, use <code>FakeTimeProvider</code> (from <code>Microsoft.Extensions.TimeProvider.Testing</code>) to control "now".</li>
<li dir="auto">This makes time-dependent logic (expiry checks, scheduling, TTL calculations) fully deterministic in tests.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: requires changing existing code that calls <code>DateTime.UtcNow</code> directly — a one-time refactor cost that pays off in every time-sensitive test.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Unit Testing.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Unit Testing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Unit Testing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you NOT write unit tests?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Trivial property getters/setters with no logic — the test adds noise without catching real bugs.</li>
<li dir="auto">UI rendering logic — visual correctness is better verified with snapshot tests or manual QA.</li>
<li dir="auto">Infrastructure wiring (DI registration, config parsing) — test this with integration tests that boot the real container.</li>
<li dir="auto">Exploratory spike code — write the spike without tests, learn from it, then delete it before it becomes production code.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: every test has a maintenance cost. Tests that don't catch real bugs are pure overhead. Focus unit tests on logic with branching, edge cases, and business rules.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Unit Testing.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Paradigms/Unit Testing.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Unit Testing</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="06-development-practices-principles">Principles</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you distinguish 'simple' from 'simplistic' in a design review?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Simple: the design has the minimum number of moving parts needed to meet the current requirements, with clear failure modes and no hidden assumptions. Simplistic: the design ignores real requirements (edge cases, error handling, security) to appear simple. The test: can you explain every component's purpose? If a component exists 'just in case' or 'for future flexibility,' it is probably over-engineering. If a component is missing and the system fails in production, it was simplistic.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/KISS.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/KISS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">KISS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is complexity justified despite KISS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the complexity solves a proven, current problem: security controls (rate limiting, input validation, authentication) are mandatory for public APIs; retry logic and circuit breakers are mandatory for distributed systems; idempotency keys are mandatory for payment processing. The principle is: add complexity only to solve a proven problem, not a hypothetical one. Complexity that prevents production failures is not over-engineering.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/KISS.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/KISS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">KISS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does KISS interact with YAGNI and DRY?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">They are complementary: YAGNI says don't build features you don't need yet; DRY says don't duplicate knowledge; KISS says keep the implementation simple. Tension arises when DRY requires an abstraction that adds complexity (KISS violation) for a single use case (YAGNI violation). Resolution: apply the Rule of Three — abstract when you have two concrete use cases, not one. One use case is speculation; two give you enough information to design a simple abstraction.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/KISS.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/KISS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">KISS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between IoC and Dependency Injection?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">IoC is the principle: the framework controls object creation and wiring instead of your code. Dependency Injection is the most common technique for implementing IoC: dependencies are passed in (injected) rather than created internally. You can implement IoC without DI (e.g., using a service locator or factory), but constructor injection via a DI container is the idiomatic .NET approach. IoC is the what; DI is the how.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/IoC (Holywood Principle).md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/IoC (Holywood Principle).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">IoC (Holywood Principle)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between IoC and the Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">DIP (SOLID 'D') is a design rule: high-level modules should depend on abstractions, not concrete implementations. IoC is a runtime mechanism: the framework wires concrete implementations to those abstractions. DIP is the why (depend on interfaces); IoC/DI is the how (let the container provide the concrete class). You can follow DIP without a DI container by manually wiring dependencies in Main, but a container makes it practical at scale.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/IoC (Holywood Principle).md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/IoC (Holywood Principle).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">IoC (Holywood Principle)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is DRY actually trying to prevent?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Duplicated knowledge and duplicated rules.<br>
If a change requires edits in multiple places, DRY is a signal.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/DRY.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/DRY.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">DRY</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is it OK to repeat code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the repetition is small, the meaning is different, or the logic is expected to diverge.<br>
Local duplication is sometimes safer than a shared abstraction.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/DRY.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/DRY.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">DRY</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which SOLID principles does a typical Singleton violate?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">DIP: code depends on a concrete global instance instead of an abstraction injected via DI.</li>
<li dir="auto">SRP: the singleton often mixes business logic with lifecycle and global access concerns.</li>
<li dir="auto">OCP: replacing/extending behavior usually requires changing call sites or the singleton itself.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix: expose an interface and let a DI container manage a singleton lifetime.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: DI adds indirection; singletons are simpler but harder to test and extend.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/SOLID.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/SOLID.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SOLID</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is it acceptable to violate SOLID principles?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Small scripts and prototypes where the overhead of abstractions exceeds the benefit.</li>
<li dir="auto">Performance-critical paths where virtual dispatch or interface overhead is measurable.</li>
<li dir="auto">When the "correct" abstraction is not yet clear — premature abstraction is worse than duplication.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: SOLID reduces coupling and improves testability at the cost of more files, more indirection, and more cognitive overhead.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/SOLID.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/SOLID.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SOLID</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Does YAGNI mean you should skip tests and refactoring?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. YAGNI applies to features and abstractions, not to engineering practices. Martin Fowler explicitly distinguishes: YAGNI says don't build features you don't need yet. It does not say skip tests, skip refactoring, or write messy code. Good engineering practices are always justified because they reduce the cost of future changes.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/YAGNI.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/YAGNI.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">YAGNI</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When does YAGNI conflict with good design, and how do you resolve it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">YAGNI conflicts with design when adding an abstraction now would make future changes cheaper. The resolution: add the abstraction when you have two concrete use cases, not one. One use case is speculation; two use cases give you enough information to design the abstraction correctly. This is the Rule of Three from refactoring.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/YAGNI.md" href="Software Engineering/06 Development Practices/Principles/YAGNI.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">YAGNI</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="07-security">07 Security</h1><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is Block-chain?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A blockchain is an append-only ledger where records are grouped into blocks and linked together using cryptographic hashes. Changing a past block changes its hash, which breaks the chain unless all subsequent blocks are recomputed and the network's consensus rules are satisfied.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Block-chain.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Block-chain.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Block-chain</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is blockchain justified over a traditional database?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Blockchain is justified when you need a shared ledger across mutually distrusting parties with no central authority and no single party can be trusted to maintain the record. If all parties trust a central authority, a traditional database with append-only audit logging is simpler, faster, and GDPR-compliant. The cost of blockchain — low throughput, immutability conflicts with erasure rights, consensus overhead — is only worth paying when decentralization is a hard requirement.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Block-chain.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Block-chain.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Block-chain</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does PoW waste energy and what is the alternative?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Proof-of-Work requires miners to perform computationally expensive hash searches to earn the right to add a block. This energy expenditure is the security mechanism — attacking the chain requires outspending honest miners. Proof-of-Stake replaces energy expenditure with economic stake: validators lock up tokens as collateral and lose them if they act dishonestly. PoS achieves similar security guarantees at a fraction of the energy cost, which is why Ethereum migrated from PoW to PoS in 2022.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Block-chain.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Block-chain.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Block-chain</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does signing use the private key to encrypt the hash, not the public key?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The private key is secret — only the signer can produce a valid signature. Anyone with the public key can verify it, but only the private key holder can create it. Reversing this would let anyone forge signatures.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Digital Signature.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Digital Signature.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Digital Signature</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between signing and encryption?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Signing proves authenticity and integrity — it does not hide the content. Encryption hides the content but does not prove who sent it. TLS uses both: the server certificate is signed (proves identity), and the session data is encrypted (provides confidentiality).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Digital Signature.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Digital Signature.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Digital Signature</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is ECDSA preferred over RSA for new systems?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">ECDSA produces shorter signatures (256-bit key gives equivalent security to 3072-bit RSA) and is faster to compute. RSA is still widely used for compatibility, but ECDSA is the modern default for JWT signing (ES256) and TLS certificates.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Digital Signature.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Digital Signature.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Digital Signature</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use symmetric vs asymmetric encryption?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use symmetric (AES-256-GCM) for bulk data encryption — it is 10-100x faster than asymmetric. Use asymmetric (RSA, ECDH) for key exchange and digital signatures, not for encrypting data directly. In practice, use hybrid encryption: asymmetric to exchange a symmetric key, then symmetric for the data. This is exactly what TLS does.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Encryption.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Encryption.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Encryption</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is envelope encryption and when is it used?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Envelope encryption uses two keys: a Data Encryption Key (DEK) encrypts the data; a Key Encryption Key (KEK) encrypts the DEK. The encrypted DEK is stored alongside the ciphertext. The KEK lives in a key management service (Azure Key Vault, AWS KMS) and never leaves it. This pattern is used in cloud storage (Azure Blob Storage, S3 server-side encryption) and allows key rotation without re-encrypting all data — you only re-encrypt the DEK.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Encryption.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Encryption.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Encryption</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is key management harder than choosing the right algorithm?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The algorithm (AES-256-GCM) is well-understood and rarely the weak point. Key management is where systems fail: hardcoded keys in source code, keys stored in plaintext config files, no key rotation policy, no audit trail for key access. Use Azure Key Vault or AWS KMS to store keys, rotate them automatically, and audit every access. Never derive keys from passwords without a proper KDF (PBKDF2, Argon2).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Encryption.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Encryption.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Encryption</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a JWT token and why is it not encrypted by default?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">JWT is a compact token format: <code>header.payload.signature</code> (base64url encoded).</li>
<li dir="auto">Signed (JWS) means the signature proves integrity and issuer, but the payload is readable by anyone with the token.</li>
<li dir="auto">Encryption (JWE) is a separate standard that wraps the JWT in an encrypted envelope.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: signed-only JWTs are simpler and faster to validate; JWE adds encryption overhead and key management complexity.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/JWT Bearer.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/JWT Bearer.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">JWT Bearer</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why should JWTs have short expiry times?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">JWTs are stateless — the server cannot revoke them without a blacklist (which defeats the stateless benefit).</li>
<li dir="auto">A stolen JWT is valid until it expires. Short expiry (15-60 min) limits the damage window.</li>
<li dir="auto">Refresh tokens (stored server-side) allow issuing new JWTs without re-authentication.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: short expiry requires refresh token infrastructure; long expiry is simpler but riskier.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/JWT Bearer.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/JWT Bearer.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">JWT Bearer</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which OWASP Top 10 item is most commonly found in production .NET apps?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">A01 (Broken Access Control) is the most prevalent — 94% of tested apps had at least one instance.</li>
<li dir="auto">Common .NET manifestation: missing <code>[Authorize]</code> attributes, IDOR (insecure direct object references) where IDs are not validated against the current user.</li>
<li dir="auto">A05 (Security Misconfiguration) is also common: developer exception pages in production, missing security headers.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: fixing access control requires understanding the authorization model, not just adding attributes — resource-based authorization is more work but more correct.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/OWASP.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/OWASP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OWASP</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you prevent SQL injection in a .NET application?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use parameterized queries always — EF Core and Dapper with parameters are safe by default.</li>
<li dir="auto">Never concatenate user input into SQL strings, even for dynamic ORDER BY or table names.</li>
<li dir="auto">For dynamic SQL, use allowlists (validate column names against a known set) rather than sanitization.</li>
<li dir="auto">Enable SQL Server's Query Store to detect unusual query patterns.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: ORMs prevent injection but can generate inefficient queries — use raw parameterized SQL for performance-critical paths.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/OWASP.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/OWASP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OWASP</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="07-security-authentication">Authentication</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is Basic Auth unsafe over HTTP?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Base64 is encoding, not encryption — it is trivially reversible. Over HTTP, the <code>Authorization</code> header is sent in plaintext and visible to any network observer. Over HTTPS, the header is encrypted by TLS, making Basic Auth safe to use.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Basic Auth.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Basic Auth.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Basic Auth</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is Basic Auth acceptable in production?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">For machine-to-machine calls between trusted services on an internal network over HTTPS, Basic Auth is acceptable when simplicity matters and the credential is a service account (not a user password). For user-facing authentication, use OAuth 2.0 / JWT Bearer — Basic Auth requires sending credentials on every request, which increases exposure.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Basic Auth.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Basic Auth.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Basic Auth</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between OAuth 2.0 and OpenID Connect?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">OAuth 2.0 is an authorization framework: it grants access to resources (what you can do).</li>
<li dir="auto">OIDC is an authentication layer on top of OAuth 2.0: it proves who the user is.</li>
<li dir="auto">OAuth 2.0 issues access tokens; OIDC additionally issues ID tokens (JWTs with user claims).</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: OIDC adds the ID token and UserInfo endpoint; OAuth 2.0 alone cannot tell you who the user is.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Oauth OIDC (OpenId Connect).md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Oauth OIDC (OpenId Connect).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Oauth OIDC (OpenId Connect)</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between role-based and resource-based authorization?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Role-based authorization checks what type of user you are (e.g., Admin, Editor). Resource-based authorization checks your relationship to a specific resource instance (e.g., are you the owner of this document?). Use role-based for coarse-grained access control; use resource-based when the decision depends on data.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Resource-based Auth.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Resource-based Auth.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Resource-based Auth</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why inject <code>IAuthorizationService</code> instead of checking ownership in the controller directly?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>IAuthorizationService</code> centralizes authorization logic in handlers, making it testable and reusable across controllers. Direct ownership checks in controllers scatter authorization logic, making it easy to miss a check or apply it inconsistently. The handler pattern also supports multiple requirements composing into a single policy.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Resource-based Auth.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Resource-based Auth.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Resource-based Auth</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What problem does SSO solve and what does it not solve?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">SSO eliminates repeated interactive logins across applications — users authenticate once and access many services. It does not replace per-application authorization (what the user can do) or per-application session management (how long the session lasts). Each application still manages its own session and access control.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/SSO (Single Sign-On).md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/SSO (Single Sign-On).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SSO (Single Sign-On)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What must you validate when accepting an ID token from an IdP?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Signature (using IdP's public key), issuer, audience (your client_id), expiry, and nonce. Also validate the redirect URI matches the registered value. Missing any of these opens the door to token forgery, replay attacks, or open redirect vulnerabilities.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/SSO (Single Sign-On).md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/SSO (Single Sign-On).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SSO (Single Sign-On)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose SAML over OIDC?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When integrating with legacy enterprise IdPs that only support SAML (older ADFS, some SaaS products). For new systems, OIDC is simpler, better supported on mobile/SPA, and has a richer ecosystem of libraries. The cost of SAML: XML parsing complexity, larger message sizes, and harder debugging.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/SSO (Single Sign-On).md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/SSO (Single Sign-On).md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SSO (Single Sign-On)</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is FIDO2/WebAuthn more secure than TOTP?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">TOTP codes can be phished — an attacker can trick a user into entering their code on a fake site. FIDO2 keys are bound to the origin domain: the browser only uses the key for the exact domain it was registered on, making phishing impossible. The private key also never leaves the device.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Two-Factor Auth.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Two-Factor Auth.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Two-Factor Auth</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use TOTP vs FIDO2?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">TOTP is simpler to implement and works on any device with an authenticator app — use it for most applications. FIDO2 is phishing-resistant and required for high-assurance scenarios (banking, admin access, NIST AAL3). The cost is hardware dependency and more complex enrollment flow.<br>
