<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Two-Factor Auth.md" href="Software Engineering/07 Security/Authentication/Two-Factor Auth.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Two-Factor Auth</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="08-sdlc">08 SDLC</h1><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain Agile, Scrum, Kanban, and Waterfall.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Agile: an umbrella of values and principles (iterative delivery, feedback, adapting to change).</li>
<li dir="auto">Scrum: an Agile framework with defined roles (Product Owner, Scrum Master, Developers), events (sprint planning, daily scrum, review, retrospective), and artifacts (product backlog, sprint backlog, increment).</li>
<li dir="auto">Kanban: a flow-based method focused on visualizing work, limiting work in progress, and optimizing lead time/throughput.</li>
<li dir="auto">Waterfall: a sequential lifecycle (requirements -&gt; design -&gt; implementation -&gt; testing -&gt; release) that works best when requirements are stable and change is expensive.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/08 SDLC.md" href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/08 SDLC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">08 SDLC</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When does BDD overhead outweigh its benefits?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">BDD adds overhead: Gherkin scenarios must be written and maintained, step definitions must be kept in sync, and the collaboration process takes time. The overhead is not worth it for: pure technical components with no business language (algorithms, infrastructure), small teams where developers and stakeholders communicate directly without formal scenarios, and rapidly changing requirements where maintaining <code>.feature</code> files becomes a burden. BDD earns its cost when: business rules are complex and misunderstanding them is expensive, cross-functional alignment is genuinely needed, and scenarios serve as living documentation.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/BDD.md" href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/BDD.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">BDD</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does BDD differ from TDD, and are they complementary?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">TDD focuses on unit behavior: does the code work correctly? Tests are written by developers in code. BDD focuses on system behavior: does the system meet requirements from the user's perspective? Scenarios are written in business language (Gherkin) collaboratively with stakeholders. They are complementary: use TDD for unit-level design and fast feedback loops; use BDD for acceptance criteria and cross-functional alignment. A well-tested system uses both — TDD for the inner loop, BDD for the outer loop.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/BDD.md" href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/BDD.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">BDD</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is 'specification by example' and how does it reduce requirement ambiguity?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Specification by example replaces abstract requirement statements ('the system should handle invalid input') with concrete examples ('given a blank email field, when I submit the form, then I see the error message X'). Concrete examples are unambiguous: they can be automated as tests and verified against the running system. Abstract requirements are interpreted differently by developers, testers, and business stakeholders. The Three Amigos practice (developer + tester + business) produces examples collaboratively, surfacing misunderstandings before implementation begins.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/BDD.md" href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/BDD.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">BDD</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why are story points measured in relative complexity rather than hours?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Hours are absolute and vary by person, day, and context. A senior engineer estimates 2 hours; a junior estimates 8 hours for the same task. Story points measure relative complexity: a 5-point story is roughly twice as complex as a 2-point story, regardless of who does it. A team's velocity (points per sprint) emerges from historical data and automatically accounts for team-specific factors like skill level, interruptions, and tech debt. Treating story points as hours destroys this abstraction and makes velocity meaningless.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/Estimation Techniques.md" href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/Estimation Techniques.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Estimation Techniques</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the most common estimation mistake and how do you avoid it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Treating story points as hours ('8 points = 8 hours'). This forces estimators to think in absolute time, reintroduces the person-dependency problem, and makes velocity comparisons between teams meaningless. The fix: calibrate story points against reference stories ('this is a 2-point story because it's similar to the login feature we built last sprint'), track velocity over 3-5 sprints to establish a baseline, and use that baseline for capacity planning — never convert points to hours.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/Estimation Techniques.md" href="Software Engineering/08 SDLC/Estimation Techniques.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Estimation Techniques</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="09-devops">09 DevOps</h1><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use GitHub Actions vs Azure DevOps Pipelines?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use GitHub Actions when your code is on GitHub and you want zero infrastructure overhead — it integrates natively with PRs, branch protection, and OIDC for cloud auth. Use Azure DevOps Pipelines when deploying to Azure services (AKS, App Service, Azure Functions) and needing enterprise features: approval gates, deployment environments, audit trails, and integration with Azure Boards. Both support .NET equally well; the decision is about ecosystem fit and operational requirements.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/CI CD tools.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/CI CD tools.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CI CD tools</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes a CI pipeline 'good' vs 'fast but unreliable'?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A good CI pipeline: (1) runs in &lt; 10 minutes (fast enough to not block the developer), (2) has zero flaky tests (every failure is a real failure), (3) masks secrets and never logs sensitive data, (4) promotes the same artifact through environments (no rebuilds), (5) has clear failure messages that point to the root cause. A fast but unreliable pipeline trains developers to ignore failures and re-run instead of fix — which defeats the purpose of CI.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/CI CD tools.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/CI CD tools.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">CI CD tools</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between a Docker image and a container?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">An image is a read-only layered filesystem built from a Dockerfile. It is immutable.</li>
<li dir="auto">A container is a running instance of an image with a writable layer on top.</li>
<li dir="auto">Multiple containers can run from the same image simultaneously.</li>
<li dir="auto">Deleting a container does not delete the image.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: images are shared and cached; containers are ephemeral and isolated.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Docker.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Docker.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Docker</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why use multi-stage builds for .NET applications?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The .NET SDK image (~800MB) includes compilers and build tools not needed at runtime.</li>
<li dir="auto">The ASP.NET runtime image (~200MB) is sufficient for running the published app.</li>
<li dir="auto">Multi-stage builds copy only the published output to the final image, reducing size by ~75%.</li>
<li dir="auto">Smaller images mean faster pulls, less attack surface, and lower registry storage costs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Cost: slightly more complex Dockerfile; worth it for any production workload.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Docker.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Docker.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Docker</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you prevent secrets from leaking into Docker images?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Never use <code>ENV</code> or <code>ARG</code> for secrets in Dockerfiles — they are visible in <code>docker history</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Pass secrets at runtime via environment variables (<code>docker run -e</code>) or Docker secrets.</li>
<li dir="auto">For build-time secrets (e.g., NuGet feeds), use <code>RUN --mount=type=secret</code> (BuildKit).</li>
<li dir="auto">In Kubernetes, use Secrets mounted as environment variables or files.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: runtime injection adds operational complexity but is the only safe approach.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Docker.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Docker.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Docker</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between a Pod, Deployment, and Service?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Pod: the smallest unit; wraps one or more containers sharing a network namespace.</li>
<li dir="auto">Deployment: manages a set of identical pods; handles rolling updates and scaling.</li>
<li dir="auto">Service: stable network endpoint (DNS name + IP) that load-balances across pods.</li>
<li dir="auto">Pods are ephemeral (new IP on restart); Services provide stable addressing.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: Deployments add a layer of abstraction over pods, but that abstraction enables zero-downtime updates.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Kubernetes.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Kubernetes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Kubernetes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do Kubernetes Secrets need additional protection beyond base64 encoding?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Base64 is encoding, not encryption — anyone with etcd access can decode secrets trivially.</li>
<li dir="auto">By default, etcd stores secrets in plaintext (base64 is just a transport format).</li>
<li dir="auto">Mitigations: enable etcd encryption at rest, use Sealed Secrets (encrypted in git), or use Azure Key Vault with the Secrets Store CSI driver.</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: external secret management adds operational complexity but is required for regulated workloads.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Kubernetes.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Kubernetes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Kubernetes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What causes CrashLoopBackOff and how do you debug it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">CrashLoopBackOff means the container starts, crashes, and Kubernetes keeps restarting it with exponential backoff.</li>
<li dir="auto">Common causes: app crashes on startup (missing config, DB connection failure), liveness probe failing before app is ready, OOMKilled (memory limit too low).</li>
<li dir="auto">Debug: <code>kubectl logs &lt;pod&gt; --previous</code> (logs from the crashed instance), <code>kubectl describe pod &lt;pod&gt;</code> (events and exit codes), <code>kubectl exec -it &lt;pod&gt; -- /bin/sh</code> (if the container starts briefly).</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: liveness probes are essential for self-healing but misconfigured probes cause the problem they are meant to solve.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Kubernetes.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Kubernetes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Kubernetes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you diagnose an intermittent p95 or p99 latency bottleneck in a multi-service system?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start from traces to find which span dominates p95/p99 latency on slow requests.</li>
<li dir="auto">Correlate with RED metrics per service to confirm whether latency is isolated or systemic.</li>
<li dir="auto">Check dependency spans (DB, cache, external API) to identify downstream propagation.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use structured logs with the same trace ID to validate edge-case payloads or retries.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why this answer is strong: it uses all three pillars together instead of guessing from one dashboard.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Observability.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Observability.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Observability</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use RED vs USE in a distributed system interview design?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use RED for customer-facing service endpoints and request pipelines.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use USE for underlying resources such as CPU, thread pools, queue depth, and DB connections.</li>
<li dir="auto">Combine both: RED shows symptom at service boundary; USE explains pressure source beneath it.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why this answer is strong: it separates service health from resource health and links them causally.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Observability.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Observability.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Observability</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why instrument with OpenTelemetry from day one instead of adding observability later?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Early instrumentation bakes telemetry into contracts and code paths before scale complexity appears.</li>
<li dir="auto">Retrofitting often misses historical baselines and requires invasive code changes across many services.</li>
<li dir="auto">Vendor-neutral instrumentation preserves backend flexibility as platform needs evolve.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why this answer is strong: it explains operational risk, engineering cost, and architecture flexibility.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Observability.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Observability.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Observability</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="09-devops-deployment-strategies">Deployment Strategies</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is abc?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Answer<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Deployment Strategies/Deployment Strategies.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Deployment Strategies/Deployment Strategies.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deployment Strategies</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is abc?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Answer<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Deployment Strategies/Deployment Strategies.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Deployment Strategies/Deployment Strategies.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deployment Strategies</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="09-devops-version-control-systems">Version Control Systems</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does GitFlow create integration problems for teams practicing continuous deployment?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">GitFlow's long-lived <code>develop</code> branch accumulates divergence from <code>main</code> over days or weeks. Feature branches branch off <code>develop</code>, so they also diverge. When multiple features merge back, conflicts compound. The <code>release/*</code> stabilization phase adds a manual gate that prevents continuous deployment. For web services that deploy multiple times per day, GitFlow's overhead is pure cost with no benefit. Use trunk-based development or GitHub Flow instead.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Version Control Systems/Branching Stratagies.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Version Control Systems/Branching Stratagies.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Branching Stratagies</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does trunk-based development require that makes it unsuitable for all teams?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Trunk-based development requires: (1) feature flags to hide incomplete features from users, (2) fast CI (&lt; 10 minutes) so broken commits are caught quickly, (3) team discipline to keep commits small and the trunk green. Without feature flags, incomplete features must be fully hidden behind code-level conditions or held back entirely. Without fast CI, a broken commit blocks the whole team. Teams without these practices should start with GitHub Flow (short-lived PRs) and evolve toward trunk-based as their CI matures.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Version Control Systems/Branching Stratagies.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Version Control Systems/Branching Stratagies.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Branching Stratagies</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a version control system (VCS)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A VCS is a tool that tracks changes to files over time and lets you:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">keep a history of commits (who changed what, when, and why)</li>
<li dir="auto">collaborate safely (pull, push, resolve conflicts)</li>
<li dir="auto">work in parallel via branches and merge changes back</li>
<li dir="auto">tag/release specific versions and roll back when needed<br>
In practice, Git is the most common distributed VCS.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Version Control Systems/Version Control Systems.md" href="Software Engineering/09 DevOps/Version Control Systems/Version Control Systems.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Version Control Systems</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h1 id="10-cloud">10 Cloud</h1><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the key operational difference between IaaS and PaaS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">With IaaS you manage the OS, runtime, and middleware — you patch the OS, configure security groups, and handle scaling policies. With PaaS the provider manages all of that; you only deploy application code and data. The tradeoff: IaaS gives full control (custom OS, GPU, specific runtime versions); PaaS eliminates operational overhead at the cost of flexibility. Start with PaaS for new web applications; move to IaaS only when PaaS cannot meet your requirements.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/10 Cloud/IaaS, PaaS, SaaS, CaaS.md" href="Software Engineering/10 Cloud/IaaS, PaaS, SaaS, CaaS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">IaaS, PaaS, SaaS, CaaS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Where does CaaS fit between IaaS and PaaS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">CaaS (e.g., AKS, EKS) sits between them: you manage container images and deployments, but the provider manages the Kubernetes control plane and underlying OS. It gives more control than PaaS (you define your own container images, resource limits, and networking) while eliminating the Kubernetes control plane management burden of IaaS. Use CaaS when you need container portability or multi-service orchestration that PaaS cannot provide.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/10 Cloud/IaaS, PaaS, SaaS, CaaS.md" href="Software Engineering/10 Cloud/IaaS, PaaS, SaaS, CaaS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">IaaS, PaaS, SaaS, CaaS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="11-ai-ml">11 AI &amp; ML</h1><h2 id="11-ai-ml-llm">LLM</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How can Matryoshka dimensionality reduction lower embedding storage costs without significant recall loss?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use the <code>dimensions</code> parameter to truncate vectors to 256 or 512 dimensions. Models trained with Matryoshka objectives produce vectors where any prefix is independently meaningful. Truncation to 256 dims reduces storage by ~6x and speeds up ANN search proportionally. Validate by comparing recall@k on your evaluation set at 256, 512, and full dimensions — MRL models can retain strong retrieval quality at reduced dimensions, but the actual degradation is model-specific and corpus-dependent. If recall drops unacceptably, use a two-stage approach: retrieve at reduced dimensions, then re-rank candidates using full-dimensional vectors.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Embeddings.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Embeddings.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Embeddings</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can switching to a higher-scoring embedding model cause recall to drop on existing queries?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Different embedding models produce vectors in different geometric spaces — cosine similarity between vectors from two different models is meaningless. If the corpus is not re-embedded with the new model, queries encoded with the new model are compared against vectors from the old model, producing degraded or random rankings. The fix is to re-embed the entire corpus with the new model before switching query-time inference. This is also why embedding caches must key by model name and version.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Embeddings.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Embeddings.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Embeddings</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is domain-finetuning the embedding model justified over improving chunking or retrieval?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Finetuning is justified when retrieval failures are specifically caused by semantic mismatches on domain terminology — queries and relevant documents contain the same domain concepts but land far apart in vector space. If failures trace to split logical units (chunking issue), missing metadata filters (retrieval pipeline issue), or query ambiguity (query translation issue), finetuning the embedding model will not help. Diagnose by examining the top-k retrieved chunks for failed queries: if semantically relevant chunks exist but rank low, the embedding model is the bottleneck.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Embeddings.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Embeddings.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Embeddings</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is adjusting temperature and top_p simultaneously discouraged?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Both reshape the same token probability distribution but through different mechanisms. Temperature scales the logit distribution (sharpening or flattening), while top_p truncates it to a cumulative mass threshold. Changing both creates unpredictable interactions — a low temperature already concentrates probability mass, so a low top_p on top of it may have no additional effect, while a high temperature with a low top_p creates conflicting signals. Tuning one while keeping the other at default keeps behavior predictable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Generation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Generation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Generation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can a grounded response still contain unsupported claims despite citation tags?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Models can attach citation markers to claims without verifying entailment. The citation looks correct but the cited passage may not actually support the claim — it may be topically related but not evidentially sufficient. This is why citation generation alone is not grounding: a separate claim-to-source verification step (NLI or similar) is needed to confirm that each cited passage actually entails the claim it is attached to.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Generation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Generation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Generation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should constrained decoding be preferred over JSON mode for structured output?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Constrained decoding enforces a specific JSON schema at the token level during generation — the output is structurally compliant by construction. JSON mode only guarantees valid JSON without schema enforcement, so the model can return any valid JSON structure. Use constrained decoding when downstream systems depend on a specific schema (API contracts, database inserts, tool arguments). Use JSON mode when you want parsable output but the structure is flexible or exploratory.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Generation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Generation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Generation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the minimum useful guardrail set for a production LLM application?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">(1) Allowlisted tools/actions only, (2) strict output schema validation, (3) PII scanning on outputs, (4) prompt injection detection on inputs, (5) an abstention/escalation path for uncertainty. These five controls catch the most common failure modes at low cost. Add content safety classifiers and human-in-the-loop for high-risk domains.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Guardrails.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Guardrails.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Guardrails</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can you rely on a safety filter alone?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. Filters help with known bad patterns but miss novel attacks, indirect injections, and context-dependent harms. You still need least-privilege tool access, structured outputs, and tests for injection and data exfiltration. Defense in depth is the only reliable approach.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Guardrails.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Guardrails.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Guardrails</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you test guardrails?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Build a red-team test suite: injection attempts ("ignore all previous instructions"), jailbreaks ("you are DAN"), data exfiltration attempts, and out-of-scope requests. Run the suite on every model or prompt change. Track pass rates over time — a regression in the red-team suite is a deployment blocker.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Guardrails.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Guardrails.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Guardrails</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is LLM?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A large language model (LLM) is a neural network trained on large corpora of text to predict the next token. Once trained, it can be adapted to tasks like answering questions, summarizing, writing, and tool use by conditioning on instructions and context.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/LLM.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/LLM.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LLM</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>Agents</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does the ReAct pattern outperform chain-of-thought reasoning alone for tasks requiring external knowledge?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Chain-of-thought generates reasoning traces but has no mechanism to verify claims against external reality. When the model encounters a factual question it must rely on parametric memory, which produces confident but fabricated answers. ReAct interleaves reasoning with tool calls — the model can search, look up, or compute before continuing its chain. This grounds each step in real data, cutting hallucination. The original paper showed this on HotpotQA: CoT alone frequently hallucinated intermediate facts, while ReAct retrieved them. The tradeoff is latency and cost — each tool call adds a round trip and tokens.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agent Loop.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agent Loop.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agent Loop</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the three most critical production safeguards for an agent loop?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ol>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Iteration cap</strong> — prevents infinite loops and cost runaway. Without a hard limit a confused agent loops indefinitely. Set per-request, not globally. 2. <strong>Token budget tracking</strong> — each iteration grows the context. Track cumulative tokens and terminate or summarize before hitting the window limit. Without this the model silently loses earlier context and quality degrades. 3. <strong>Tool call validation</strong> — validate function names and arguments against the schema before execution. A hallucinated tool call that passes through can trigger unintended side effects — database writes, payments, external API calls. Return errors to the model so it can self-correct.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agent Loop.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agent Loop.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agent Loop</a></em></li>
</ol>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is a simple prompt chain preferable to an agent loop?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the task decomposes into a fixed, predictable sequence of steps. A prompt chain — step A then validate then step B then validate then step C — is cheaper, faster, more debuggable, and produces more consistent results. The agent loop adds value only when you cannot predict the steps in advance: the model must decide dynamically which tools to call and in what order based on intermediate results. Most production systems that call themselves agents are actually prompt chains, and that is the right choice for the majority of use cases.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agent Loop.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agent Loop.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agent Loop</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you use a workflow instead of an autonomous agent?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use a workflow when the task decomposes into predictable steps with clear inputs and outputs at each stage</li>
<li dir="auto">Workflows are cheaper, faster, and more debuggable than autonomous agents</li>
<li dir="auto">Use an autonomous agent only when steps are unpredictable, the task is open-ended, and you have feedback mechanisms (tests, eval criteria) to catch errors</li>
<li dir="auto">Most production "agents" are actually workflows — and that is the right choice for the majority of use cases</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: workflows trade flexibility for reliability; agents trade reliability for adaptability<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agents.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agents.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agents</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does Anthropic recommend starting with the simplest possible solution?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Each layer of complexity — chaining, routing, parallelization, autonomy — adds latency, cost, and failure surface</li>
<li dir="auto">A single well-prompted LLM call with good retrieval solves many tasks without orchestration</li>
<li dir="auto">Adding orchestration only makes sense when you can demonstrate measurably better outcomes</li>
<li dir="auto">Teams that start with complex multi-agent systems often spend more time debugging coordination than solving the actual problem</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: simpler systems are easier to debug and cheaper to run, but cannot handle tasks requiring dynamic decision-making<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agents.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agents.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agents</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is tool design often more important than prompt design in agentic systems?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">In a single LLM call, the prompt is the entire interface; in an agentic system, the LLM interacts with tools repeatedly</li>
<li dir="auto">Each tool call is a decision point where the agent can succeed or fail — errors compound across steps</li>
<li dir="auto">Poorly documented tools, ambiguous parameters, or inconsistent error messages cause wrong tool calls</li>
<li dir="auto">Anthropic's SWE-bench team spent more time optimizing tools (e.g., absolute vs relative file paths) than prompts, because tool quality directly determined success rate</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: investing in tool interface quality yields compounding returns across all agent interactions, while prompt improvements affect only the initial call<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agents.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Agents.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agents</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does MCP use a host-client-server architecture instead of letting the LLM talk to servers directly?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The host mediates all communication between the LLM and MCP servers. This is a deliberate security boundary — the host can enforce policies (which tools the model can call, which resources it can read), require user approval for sensitive actions, and aggregate capabilities from multiple servers without any server being aware of the others. If the LLM talked to servers directly, there would be no central point to enforce access control, log actions, or prevent a compromised server from manipulating the model. The client-per-server model also isolates failures — one crashed server does not affect others.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Model Context Protocol.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Model Context Protocol.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Model Context Protocol</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose function calling over MCP even for a shared tool?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the tool involves sensitive business logic that should not be externalized (e.g., pricing calculations, compliance checks), when you need tight coupling between the tool and the application's internal state (e.g., in-memory session data), or when the overhead of running a separate server process is not justified. Function calling is also simpler to debug — the tool definition and implementation live in the same codebase. MCP's value is reusability across clients, so if only one client will ever use the tool, function calling is the better choice.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Model Context Protocol.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Model Context Protocol.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Model Context Protocol</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes MCP servers harder to secure than traditional REST APIs?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Three factors compound. First, tool descriptions are untrusted input that directly influences LLM behavior — a vector that traditional APIs do not have. Second, MCP servers often get broad access (database connections, file system access, API keys) because they need to fulfill diverse model requests, violating least-privilege by default. Third, the protocol lacks built-in permission scoping — OAuth authenticates and authorizes the client's access to the server, but does not restrict which specific tools or operations the client can invoke. Traditional REST APIs typically have endpoint-level authorization, rate limiting, and input validation as standard practice. MCP servers need all of these but the ecosystem is young enough that many servers ship without them.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Model Context Protocol.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Model Context Protocol.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Model Context Protocol</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is multi-agent coordination justified over a single agent with more tools?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Justified under three conditions: context pollution (subtask degrades main agent reasoning), parallelization (independent paths need concurrent execution), specialization (20+ tools degrade selection accuracy, or conflicting behavioral modes needed)</li>
<li dir="auto">If none apply, single agent wins: 3–10× fewer tokens, lower latency, single linear trace for debugging</li>
<li dir="auto">Many teams investing months in multi-agent discover equivalent results from better prompting on one agent</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: multi-agent buys context isolation and parallelism at the cost of coordination overhead and debugging complexity<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Multi-Agentic Systems.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Multi-Agentic Systems.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Multi-Agentic Systems</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does context-centric decomposition outperform problem-centric decomposition?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Problem-centric (code agent + test agent + review agent) forces constant coordination — each agent needs context from the others, creating lossy handoffs</li>
<li dir="auto">Context-centric splits along natural context boundaries — agent handling a feature also handles its tests because it already has the context</li>
<li dir="auto">Introduce a new agent only when context genuinely cannot fit in one window</li>
<li dir="auto">Reduces handoff count, cuts token overhead, prevents compounding information loss at each transfer</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: context-centric may produce broader agents (more tools per agent), but avoids the "telephone game" of multi-hop handoffs<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Multi-Agentic Systems.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Multi-Agentic Systems.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Multi-Agentic Systems</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes multi-agent failures harder to diagnose than single-agent failures?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Semantic opacity: natural language errors pass as "valid" data between agents — no schema violations, no exceptions raised. A hallucinated fact from Agent A becomes trusted input for Agent B</li>
<li dir="auto">Non-linear traces: multiple interleaving reasoning chains with handoffs instead of one sequential trace, making root cause analysis harder</li>
<li dir="auto">Emergent behavior: agent interactions produce outcomes no single agent's instructions predict</li>
<li dir="auto">Known anti-pattern: two agents entering a politeness loop, each thanking the other, consuming budget without task progress — correct behavior per agent, catastrophic in combination</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: multi-agent gains specialization but loses the single-trace debuggability that makes single-agent failures straightforward to fix<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Multi-Agentic Systems.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Multi-Agentic Systems.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Multi-Agentic Systems</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is tool design often more impactful than prompt engineering in agentic systems?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">In a single LLM call, the prompt is the entire interface — prompt quality is everything</li>
<li dir="auto">In an agentic system, the model interacts with tools across multiple loop iterations — each tool call is a decision point where errors compound</li>
<li dir="auto">Wrong tool selection, malformed arguments, or unhelpful error messages cascade across steps</li>
<li dir="auto">Anthropic's SWE-bench team spent more time improving tool interfaces than prompts, because tool quality directly determined task success rate</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: tool design effort is amortized across all agent runs; prompt tweaks are fragile and session-specific<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Tools.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Tools.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Tools</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How would you decide between one broad tool and many narrow tools?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Narrow tools reduce parameter count and ambiguity — fewer hallucinated arguments per call</li>
<li dir="auto">Too many tools degrades selection accuracy — MCPGauge measured 9.5% accuracy drop from irrelevant tool presence alone</li>
<li dir="auto">Split when a tool serves genuinely different use cases needing different descriptions and parameters</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep together when operations share context and the model would naturally chain them</li>
<li dir="auto">As tool count grows and selection confusion appears, apply routing or tool filtering to keep the active set focused</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: per-call reliability (narrow) versus selection accuracy (fewer options)<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Tools.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Tools.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Tools</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes a tool safe for caching in an agent loop versus unsafe?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Cache-safe: read-only (no side effects), deterministic output for same inputs within TTL, acceptable staleness</li>
<li dir="auto">Examples: database lookups, weather API calls, search queries — all safe with short TTLs</li>
<li dir="auto">Cache-unsafe: mutates state (create/update/delete), result depends on rapidly changing context, caching would mask failures</li>
<li dir="auto">Critical failure mode: caching a write operation returns cached "success" without executing — silent data inconsistency</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: cache hit rate and latency savings versus freshness and correctness guarantees<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Tools.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Agents/Tools.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Tools</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>Evaluation</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can deterministic checks replace LLM judges?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. They enforce format and hard rules, but they do not measure semantic correctness, relevance, or tone. A response can pass all schema checks and still be factually wrong or unhelpful. Use deterministic checks as a fast pre-filter; use LLM judges for semantic quality.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Deterministic Checks.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Deterministic Checks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deterministic Checks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the minimum useful set of deterministic checks for a tool-using agent?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">(1) Allowlisted tools/actions only, (2) strict output schema validation, (3) PII scanning on outputs, (4) injection-resistant formatting, (5) length constraints. These catch the most common failure modes cheaply before any expensive evaluation.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Deterministic Checks.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Deterministic Checks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Deterministic Checks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do I know if I'm overfitting my prompt?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">If improvements only show up on your dev set but not on a holdout set (or online metrics), you are likely tuning to that benchmark. Add paraphrased variants, segment tests, and an untouched holdout.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What should I evaluate first?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Start with the dominant failure mode that breaks user trust (often correctness/groundedness) and add targeted suites for safety risks (prompt injection, PII, unsafe actions).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When are classic metrics (BLEU/ROUGE) useful?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Mainly for narrow summarization/translation style tasks and as weak signals. For open-ended assistants, rubric-based scoring and pairwise ranking usually track real quality better.<br>
