<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How big should the golden set be?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Start small (20-50) with high signal. Grow as you discover real failures. Add targeted mini-suites for each major failure mode.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Golden Test Set and Regression Runs.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Golden Test Set and Regression Runs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Golden Test Set and Regression Runs</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do I choose what to target first?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Pick the failures that break trust or create real risk (hallucinations in high-stakes answers, data leakage, unsafe tool actions).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Golden Test Set and Regression Runs.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Golden Test Set and Regression Runs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Golden Test Set and Regression Runs</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should I prefer LLM-as-a-judge over classic metrics, and how do I know the judge is trustworthy?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use judges for open-ended generation where semantics matter and deterministic metrics (exact match, BLEU) can't capture quality.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use classic metrics for deterministic outputs or when you need hard guarantees.</li>
<li dir="auto">The key signal: if a human would need to read the answer to evaluate it, a judge model probably should too.</li>
<li dir="auto">Measure judge trustworthiness by checking agreement with a small human-labeled set.</li>
<li dir="auto">Track drift over time by re-running a fixed gold dataset after model updates.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why: judge reliability is not assumed — it must be validated and maintained like any other test harness.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/LLM-as-a-Judge.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/LLM-as-a-Judge.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LLM-as-a-Judge</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should I prefer pairwise comparisons over rubric scorecards?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Pairwise works best when iterating rapidly and the goal is "better than baseline" rather than a specific threshold.</li>
<li dir="auto">Scorecards work better when you need hard pass/fail criteria, want to track specific dimensions over time, or need to gate a release on a minimum score.</li>
<li dir="auto">Pairwise results aggregate into win-rates or Elo ratings, which are useful for comparing prompt versions or model checkpoints.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why: relative preference is cognitively easier for both humans and models than assigning an absolute score, so pairwise tends to produce more consistent verdicts on subjective quality.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/LLM-as-a-Judge.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/LLM-as-a-Judge.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LLM-as-a-Judge</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the most dangerous pitfalls when using LLM-as-a-judge in production?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Verbosity bias: judges prefer longer answers even when shorter ones are correct. Mitigate with a conciseness dimension and length caps.</li>
<li dir="auto">Position bias: in pairwise, judges favor whichever answer appears first. Always randomize A/B order.</li>
<li dir="auto">Hidden coupling: using the same model as judge and candidate inflates scores. Use a different judge model.</li>
<li dir="auto">Calibration drift: judge behavior shifts as the underlying model is updated. Maintain a gold dataset and re-run calibration periodically.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why: these biases are systematic, not random — they silently corrupt your eval signal and can cause you to ship regressions.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/LLM-as-a-Judge.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/LLM-as-a-Judge.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">LLM-as-a-Judge</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Should I rely on online metrics only?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. Use offline evaluation (golden test sets, LLM judges) to prevent obvious regressions before deployment, and online evaluation to validate real-world impact. Offline evaluation is fast and cheap; online evaluation captures real distribution and user behavior. Neither replaces the other.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Online Evaluation and AB Tests.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Online Evaluation and AB Tests.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Online Evaluation and AB Tests</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you determine the minimum sample size for an A/B test?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use a power analysis: specify the minimum detectable effect (e.g., 2% improvement in resolution rate), desired statistical power (80%), and significance level (p &lt; 0.05). Tools like statsmodels or online calculators give the required sample size. Underpowered tests produce false positives that waste engineering effort.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Online Evaluation and AB Tests.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Online Evaluation and AB Tests.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Online Evaluation and AB Tests</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between data drift and model degradation in online evaluation?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Data drift is a change in the input distribution (users asking different types of questions). Model degradation is a drop in output quality for the same input distribution. Both show up as metric regressions in online monitoring, but the remediation differs: drift may require retraining or prompt updates; degradation may indicate a model update or infrastructure issue.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Online Evaluation and AB Tests.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Evaluation/Online Evaluation and AB Tests.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Online Evaluation and AB Tests</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>Prompting</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is automated prompt optimization worth the setup cost?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">It is worth it when prompt quality directly impacts key metrics and manual iteration is too slow.</li>
<li dir="auto">You need repeatable optimization across many tasks or datasets, not one-off prompt crafting.</li>
<li dir="auto">You have an evaluation loop (datasets, acceptance metrics, or human labels) to score changes objectively.</li>
<li dir="auto">Without measurement infrastructure, automation usually adds complexity without reliable gains.<br>
Why this matters: the optimization loop only pays off when evaluation discipline exists.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Automated Prompt Optimization.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Automated Prompt Optimization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Automated Prompt Optimization</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is PAL's key insight compared to chain-of-thought prompting?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">PAL moves intermediate reasoning from free-form text to executable code.</li>
<li dir="auto">The interpreter, not the language model, performs deterministic computation.</li>
<li dir="auto">This reduces arithmetic/symbolic errors common in text-only reasoning traces.</li>
<li dir="auto">It naturally connects to tool-use agents that route subtasks to calculators, Python, or external systems.<br>
Why this matters: it separates language understanding from computation for better reliability.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Automated Prompt Optimization.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Automated Prompt Optimization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Automated Prompt Optimization</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you start with zero-shot versus few-shot?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start zero-shot for simple, well-specified tasks (basic classification, extraction, rewrite).</li>
<li dir="auto">Move to one-shot/few-shot when output format is inconsistent or class boundaries are ambiguous.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use one-shot first for lightweight steering, then increase shots only if failure patterns persist.<br>
Why this matters: it minimizes prompt complexity and token cost while improving reliability only where needed.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/In-Context Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/In-Context Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">In-Context Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What makes a good few-shot example set?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Consistent input and output structure.</li>
<li dir="auto">Coverage of real label space and edge cases.</li>
<li dir="auto">Examples representative of production distribution, not synthetic easy cases.</li>
<li dir="auto">Minimal but sufficient count (often 1-5) to show mapping clearly.<br>
Why this matters: demonstration quality usually matters more than raw quantity.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/In-Context Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/In-Context Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">In-Context Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the main failure modes of few-shot prompting?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Breakdown on tasks requiring multi-step reasoning.</li>
<li dir="auto">Sensitivity to example order and formatting drift.</li>
<li dir="auto">Context-window pressure from too many demonstrations.</li>
<li dir="auto">Poor transfer when task needs external facts not present in model knowledge.<br>
Why this matters: these limits tell you when to pivot to other techniques instead of adding more shots.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/In-Context Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/In-Context Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">In-Context Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When does Chain-of-Thought usually help, and when can it hurt?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Helps on multi-step arithmetic, logic, and planning where intermediate state tracking matters.</li>
<li dir="auto">Helps when tasks are decomposable into small correct sub-steps.</li>
<li dir="auto">Can hurt on simple factual queries by adding unnecessary tokens and extra room for drift.</li>
<li dir="auto">Can also hurt if the first bad assumption propagates through a single chain.<br>
<strong>Why:</strong> this tests whether the candidate understands mechanism-level fit, not just a slogan like "CoT is always better".<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Reasoning Techniques.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Reasoning Techniques.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Reasoning Techniques</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain the cost vs. accuracy tradeoff in self-consistency.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Accuracy often improves because multiple sampled chains reduce single-path bias.</li>
<li dir="auto">Cost and latency increase roughly with sample count N.</li>
<li dir="auto">Diminishing returns appear after a small N; tune N against SLA and budget.</li>
<li dir="auto">Works best when there is a clear way to compare or vote on final answers.<br>
<strong>Why:</strong> this checks practical engineering judgment under production constraints.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Reasoning Techniques.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Reasoning Techniques.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Reasoning Techniques</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is Tree of Thoughts justified over CoT or self-consistency?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use ToT when success requires explicit exploration of alternatives and backtracking.</li>
<li dir="auto">Typical cases: planning, combinatorial puzzles, multi-constraint decision paths.</li>
<li dir="auto">Not justified for easy tasks where CoT already reaches target quality.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose search strategy (BFS/DFS/beam) based on branching factor, depth, and compute budget.<br>
<strong>Why:</strong> this evaluates whether the candidate can map algorithmic search cost to task difficulty.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Reasoning Techniques.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/Prompting/Reasoning Techniques.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Reasoning Techniques</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>RAG</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why should retrieval cache keys be based on processed query text instead of raw embeddings?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Processed query text and transformation version are deterministic, auditable, and stable across embedding model upgrades. Raw embedding bytes are opaque, change with every model swap, and make cache invalidation on model upgrade impossible without full cache flush. Keying on processed text also aligns cache correctness with the query translation pipeline — if the translation changes, the key changes automatically.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is response caching riskier than embedding caching?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Embedding is a pure function: same text plus same model always produces the same vector. Response generation depends on the system prompt template, the retrieved evidence (which changes with index updates), the user's permissions, and the model version — all mutable. A cached response can become wrong when any of these inputs change without cache invalidation. Embedding cache entries only go stale when the source text or model version changes, which are infrequent and structurally detectable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is semantic caching safe to deploy, and when should it be avoided?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Semantic caching is safe when the domain is narrow, queries are repetitive, false positives have low cost, and you can calibrate a reliable similarity threshold on a held-out set. It should be avoided in high-stakes domains (medical, legal, financial) where a wrong cached answer causes harm, in multi-turn conversations where context changes the correct answer, and when the query distribution is too diverse to find a threshold that balances hit rate against false-positive rate.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can aggregate retrieval metrics improve while individual user segments degrade?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Aggregate metrics average across query types and tenants, masking localized regressions</li>
<li dir="auto">A pipeline change improving average Recall@5 can simultaneously degrade recall by double digits on a specific tenant's query cluster</li>
<li dir="auto">RAG workloads are heterogeneous — different query types, document formats, and languages have different retrieval characteristics</li>
<li dir="auto">The fix is segment-level evaluation: slice by tenant, language, query cluster, and document source type</li>
<li dir="auto">Flag any segment that degrades beyond the threshold, even when the aggregate improves</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: per-segment evaluation costs more to maintain (more ground-truth labels, more compute per release) but catches the most common RAG evaluation failure in production — choose granularity based on how heterogeneous your query population is<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why are relative regression thresholds preferable to absolute quality targets for RAG release gates?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Absolute thresholds are brittle across corpus changes, model updates, and workload shifts</li>
<li dir="auto">A threshold set during initial launch becomes meaningless after the corpus doubles or query distribution shifts</li>
<li dir="auto">Relative thresholds (no more than N% regression from baseline) adapt automatically because the baseline tracks the current system state</li>
<li dir="auto">They prevent the failure mode where a team sets an ambitious absolute target, cannot reach it, and disables the gate entirely</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: relative thresholds require maintaining a consistent baseline measurement across releases, which adds CI/CD complexity — but this cost is far lower than the risk of shipping regressions that absolute thresholds cannot detect after the first corpus evolution<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should a team invest in building a human-annotated golden evaluation set versus relying on synthetic QA generation?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Synthetic generation bootstraps evaluation quickly and covers breadth at low cost</li>
<li dir="auto">LLM-generated questions cluster around patterns the model finds easy to generate, missing adversarial cases, ambiguous queries, and domain-specific edge cases</li>
<li dir="auto">A golden set is worth the investment when the RAG system serves high-stakes decisions (medical, legal, financial) where evaluation failures have direct business or safety impact</li>
<li dir="auto">Golden sets also serve as regression gates — known past failures are captured permanently, preventing recurrence</li>
<li dir="auto">In practice, combine both: synthetic for broad coverage, golden for regression gating on known failure modes</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: golden sets cost annotator time (typically 2-4 hours per 100 examples) and require ongoing maintenance as the corpus evolves — invest proportionally to the cost of an undetected evaluation failure in your domain<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Given high Faithfulness (0.91) and low Context Recall (0.54), which pipeline layer do you fix first and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">High faithfulness means the model correctly uses the context it receives — generation is not the problem</li>
<li dir="auto">Low context recall means retrieval misses roughly half the necessary evidence — the retrieval layer is the bottleneck</li>
<li dir="auto">Retrieval quality is a hard ceiling on answer quality: the generator cannot use evidence it never sees</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix retrieval first: add hybrid search (BM25 + dense), expand k, review metadata filters, check embedding domain fit</li>
<li dir="auto">Do not touch prompts or generation settings until recall improves — optimizing generation against incomplete evidence is wasted effort</li>
<li dir="auto">After retrieval fix, re-measure both: if faithfulness drops as recall improves, the additional context is confusing the model — add re-ranking or improve prompt grounding</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: improving recall often decreases precision (more chunks = more noise) — pair recall improvements with re-ranking to maintain context quality<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Evaluation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is sampled LLM-as-judge scoring preferred over scoring every response in production?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Scoring every response doubles per-request cost and adds latency if synchronous.</li>
<li dir="auto">At production scale (thousands of queries/hour), full scoring is prohibitively expensive.</li>
<li dir="auto">Sampled scoring (5–20%) provides statistical coverage of the quality distribution at a fraction of the cost.</li>
<li dir="auto">Stratified sampling ensures rare but important query types (multi-hop, negation, edge-case domains) are represented.</li>
<li dir="auto">Async execution decouples scoring from user-facing latency — users never wait for the judge.</li>
<li dir="auto">Full scoring is reserved for offline evaluation runs against labeled eval sets.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Tradeoff</strong>: lower sample rates reduce cost but increase the risk of missing localized regressions in small query clusters. Start at 10–20% and reduce only with evidence that the distribution is stable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Monitoring.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Monitoring.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monitoring</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why should RAG alerting use relative regression thresholds instead of absolute quality targets?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Absolute thresholds ("faithfulness &gt; 0.9") are brittle across corpus changes, model updates, and query distribution shifts.</li>
<li dir="auto">A threshold calibrated at launch becomes meaningless after the corpus doubles or query mix evolves.</li>
<li dir="auto">Relative thresholds ("no more than 5% drop from 7-day rolling baseline") adapt automatically because the baseline tracks current system state.</li>
<li dir="auto">Relative thresholds prevent the failure mode where a team sets an ambitious absolute target, cannot reach it consistently, and silently disables the alert.</li>
<li dir="auto">Baselines must be recomputed after intentional pipeline changes (model swap, prompt update, index rebuild) to avoid false alarms on expected shifts.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Tradeoff</strong>: relative thresholds can miss slow, gradual degradation that stays within the rolling window. Complement with periodic absolute floor checks (e.g., monthly review of whether the baseline itself is still acceptable).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Monitoring.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Monitoring.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monitoring</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does monitoring differ from evaluation in a RAG system, and why do you need both?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Evaluation validates a pipeline configuration against a labeled dataset before deployment — it gates releases.</li>
<li dir="auto">Monitoring validates the pipeline continuously against live traffic after deployment — it catches production regressions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Eval sets are static snapshots; production traffic shifts continuously with new query patterns, corpus updates, and model provider changes.</li>
<li dir="auto">Monitoring catches failure modes that no static eval set anticipates: seasonal query shifts, silent model downgrades, load-dependent degradation.</li>
<li dir="auto">The feedback loop connects them: failing production traces identified by monitoring get added to the eval set, preventing recurrence in future releases.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Tradeoff</strong>: monitoring alone detects problems after users are affected; evaluation alone misses production-specific failures. Neither is sufficient without the other.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Monitoring.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Monitoring.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monitoring</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does parent-child chunking often improve answer completeness over child-only retrieval?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Child chunks maximize retrieval precision — small, focused units match specific queries well. But child-only context can miss adjacent constraints that the answer depends on (e.g., a policy rule in one child and its exception in a sibling). Parent expansion restores the surrounding context during generation, reducing partial or decontextualized answers. The tradeoff is increased context size and potential noise from the broader parent span.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Chunking.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Chunking.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Chunking</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should a team move from recursive to structure-aware chunking?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Move when retrieval errors consistently trace back to broken structural units — split tables, bisected code blocks, or separated clause/exception pairs. Recursive splitting respects paragraph boundaries but is blind to document-specific structures. Structure-aware chunking preserves those units at the cost of format-specific parser investment and ongoing parser maintenance.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Chunking.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Chunking.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Chunking</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is semantic chunking not always superior to simpler rule-based approaches?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Semantic chunking places boundaries at actual topic shifts, which sounds ideal. But it requires embedding every span during ingestion (high cost), the similarity threshold is sensitive to embedding model and domain (tuning burden), and threshold instability can cause over-fragmentation or oversized chunks. For documents with reliable structural markers (headings, sections), recursive or structure-aware chunking achieves similar boundary quality at a fraction of the ingestion cost.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Chunking.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Chunking.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Chunking</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does query translation often improve recall but sometimes hurt precision, and how do you detect the tradeoff?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Additional query variants cover vocabulary the original misses, hitting more document neighborhoods and improving recall</li>
<li dir="auto">Weak variants introduce concepts not in the original intent, pulling related-but-irrelevant documents into the candidate set</li>
<li dir="auto">Precision drop is invisible in aggregate recall metrics — recall goes up while noisy candidates are buried in the ranked list</li>
<li dir="auto">Detection: measure precision@k (not just recall@k) before and after translation, segmented by query type</li>
<li dir="auto">If recall@20 improves but precision@5 drops, translation helps coverage but hurts candidates that reach the generator</li>
<li dir="auto">Key mitigation: always include the original query as a variant and discard translations that drift too far from the original intent<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Query Translation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Query Translation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Query Translation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is decomposition a better choice than multi-query, and when does it hurt?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Decomposition fits when the original query has distinct sub-problems requiring separate evidence — comparisons, multi-entity questions, timeline questions</li>
<li dir="auto">Multi-query fits when the question has a single intent but user phrasing may not match document terminology</li>
<li dir="auto">Decomposition hurts on single-intent questions: sub-questions fragment a coherent topic and lose the constraints that make the original specific</li>
<li dir="auto">The test: if sub-questions can be answered independently and concatenated answers address the original, decomposition fits</li>
<li dir="auto">If sub-questions only make sense in context of the original question, use multi-query instead</li>
<li dir="auto">Key tradeoff: decomposition adds a synthesis LLM call and risks context fragmentation; multi-query only adds retrieval calls<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Query Translation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Query Translation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Query Translation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can HyDE outperform direct query embedding for vague questions but fail on specific factual queries?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Expected answer:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Vague queries produce sparse, underspecified embeddings equidistant from many document clusters — poor retrieval signal</li>
<li dir="auto">HyDE generates a hypothetical answer paragraph, creating a denser point in embedding space closer to real answer documents</li>
<li dir="auto">The encoder's bottleneck filters hallucinated details while preserving the correct semantic neighborhood</li>
<li dir="auto">For specific factual queries (error codes, versions), the LLM may hallucinate wrong details — different error code, different version</li>
<li dir="auto">The hallucinated embedding steers retrieval toward documents matching the wrong specifics</li>
<li dir="auto">Direct query embedding, while sparse, preserves exact tokens that keyword search in a hybrid setup can catch</li>
<li dir="auto">HyDE failure is stealth: retrieved documents look topically relevant but answer the wrong specific question<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Query Translation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Query Translation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Query Translation</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why should advanced RAG patterns be introduced incrementally instead of all at once?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Each pattern adds independent failure modes and observability needs. Incremental rollout isolates impact, allows A/B measurement against baseline, and prevents compounding complexity from masking root causes. Start with the pattern that addresses your highest-frequency failure mode.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/RAG.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/RAG.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">RAG</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is Graph RAG a better fit than plain vector retrieval?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When answers require explicit entity relations, dependency paths, or multi-hop joins that are hard to recover from independent text chunks. Examples: compliance tracing across policy documents, architecture dependency analysis, supply chain impact assessment. Skip Graph RAG for simple fact lookups where vector similarity suffices.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/RAG.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/RAG.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">RAG</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does Adaptive RAG reduce cost without sacrificing accuracy on complex queries?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A query complexity classifier routes simple queries to no-retrieval or single-pass RAG (cheap and fast) and reserves iterative multi-hop retrieval for the complex tail. Since most production queries are simple, the average cost drops significantly while complex queries still get full retrieval. Misclassification cost is asymmetric — err toward the more capable strategy when uncertain.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/RAG.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/RAG.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">RAG</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can reranking improve offline nDCG without visible quality improvement for end users?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The improvement may be in ranking positions that the generator does not use. If the generator only reads the top-3 chunks, improvements at positions 4–5 are invisible to users. Evaluate whether the reranker changes the top-k composition that actually enters the prompt, not just overall nDCG. Also verify that the eval set reflects production query distribution — gains on eval-set query types may not represent the queries users actually send.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Re-ranking.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Re-ranking.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Re-ranking</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When does reranking hurt retrieval quality instead of helping?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the reranker is out-of-distribution for your domain. A reranker trained on short web passages may misjudge relevance on long technical documents, internal terminology, or multilingual content — demoting actually relevant documents. Also when the candidate count is too small: if first-stage recall@N is already low, the reranker only reorders noise. Always compare recall and precision before and after reranking on domain-specific queries.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Re-ranking.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Re-ranking.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Re-ranking</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is ColBERT faster than a cross-encoder at query time despite also using token-level scoring?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">ColBERT pre-computes per-token document embeddings at index time and stores them. At query time, only the query tokens need encoding (one forward pass regardless of candidate count). Scoring is a MaxSim matrix operation over pre-stored vectors, not a full transformer forward pass per document. Cross-encoders must run a complete forward pass for each query-document pair because they jointly encode the concatenated input. The tradeoff is that ColBERT needs multi-vector storage, which uses more space and requires specialized indexes.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Re-ranking.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Re-ranking.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Re-ranking</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can vector-only retrieval underperform on technical support workloads?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Technical support queries often include exact identifiers: error codes, version strings, API paths, and SKU IDs. Embedding models capture meaning, not specific tokens — a query for "error E4392 in v2.3" may retrieve content about error handling in general rather than the specific code. The failure is subtle because returned chunks are topically related, so the LLM synthesizes a plausible but wrong answer. Keyword search catches these exact tokens because they are rare in the corpus (high BM25 weight), which is why hybrid retrieval is essential for these workloads.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Retrieval.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Retrieval.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Retrieval</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When does hybrid retrieval perform worse than single-mode retrieval?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When the weaker search mode contributes more noise than signal to the fused list. On homogeneous corpora where one mode dominates (e.g., scientific documents with consistent terminology and natural-language queries), the non-dominant mode pulls in marginally relevant candidates that dilute fused results. In production benchmarks, vector-only has beaten hybrid on scientific corpora because keyword search on specialized vocabulary introduced noise. Always evaluate hybrid against single-mode baselines on your actual corpus before committing to the added complexity.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Retrieval.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Retrieval.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Retrieval</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does HNSW recall degrade silently as the vector database grows?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">HNSW navigates a graph to find approximate nearest neighbors, not an exhaustive scan. The <code>ef_search</code> parameter controls how many candidate nodes the search visits. At small corpus sizes, a moderate <code>ef_search</code> finds most true neighbors. As the corpus grows, the graph becomes denser and the same <code>ef_search</code> misses more true neighbors — the search path does not explore enough of the graph to find them. Latency stays stable because the search still visits the same number of candidates, and no errors are raised. The only signal is less relevant retrieved chunks. Detection requires explicit Recall@k monitoring against a ground-truth test set.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Retrieval.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/LLM/RAG/Retrieval.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Retrieval</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="11-ai-ml-machine-learning">Machine Learning</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between data drift and concept drift?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Data drift: the input distribution P(X) changes (users ask different questions, new product categories appear). The model's learned relationship P(Y|X) may still be valid.<br>
Concept drift: the relationship P(Y|X) changes (what constitutes spam evolves, fraud patterns shift). The model's learned relationship is no longer valid — retraining is required.<br>
Cost of confusing them: retraining on new data for concept drift is necessary; retraining for benign data drift wastes resources and may reduce performance on the original distribution.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Data Drift.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Data Drift.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Data Drift</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you detect drift when labels are delayed?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use proxy metrics: escalation rate, user complaints, re-contact rate, or model confidence distributions. Monitor input feature distributions (PSI, KS test) as an early warning signal. When labels arrive, compute actual performance metrics and compare to baseline.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Data Drift.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Data Drift.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Data Drift</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How should an ML pipeline be designed to ship weekly batch churn scoring now while preserving a path to real-time scoring later?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start with batch to ship value, but define a stable feature contract and a single preprocessing implementation shared by batch and online</li>
<li dir="auto">Store features and predictions with versioned schema so you can backtest and replay later</li>
<li dir="auto">Use a model registry with stage promotion and rollback, and keep training code runnable in CI</li>
<li dir="auto">Plan the online boundary now: which features are available at request time and which require async enrichment</li>
<li dir="auto">Add monitoring from day one so you have drift and label delay visibility before moving to real time<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Machine Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Machine Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Machine Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What should be checked first when a binary classifier shows 98 percent accuracy but support tickets rise, and which metric should be optimized next?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Check class balance and the confusion matrix; high accuracy can hide poor recall on the minority class</li>
<li dir="auto">Inspect label quality and leakage sources, especially time based joins and post event signals</li>
<li dir="auto">Pick metrics based on cost: optimize precision if false positives are expensive, recall if misses are expensive, or use PR AUC for heavy imbalance</li>
<li dir="auto">Tune the decision threshold using a cost curve or expected value, not the default 0.5</li>
<li dir="auto">Run slice based evaluation to find the segments where the model fails and decide whether to collect more data or add features<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Machine Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Machine Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Machine Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What tradeoffs and rollout plan are appropriate when the best model exceeds a strict API latency budget?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Measure end to end latency budget including preprocessing, network, and tail latencies, then decide if you can meet SLO with scaling or caching</li>
<li dir="auto">Consider a smaller model, distillation, quantization, or a two stage setup where a cheap model gates the expensive one</li>
<li dir="auto">Use canary or A B rollout with guardrails on latency and key business metrics, plus automated rollback</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep a safe fallback decision path, such as a baseline model or rules, for overload and error conditions</li>
<li dir="auto">Align evaluation with production: test on representative traffic and monitor training serving skew after launch<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Machine Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Machine Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Machine Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why start every new AI feature in Shadow Mode?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Shadow Mode lets you validate accuracy on real production data without any risk of automated errors. You measure false positive rate, edge cases, and distribution shift before giving the model any authority. Skipping Shadow Mode means discovering failures in production where they have real consequences.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Spectrum Of Automations.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Spectrum Of Automations.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Spectrum Of Automations</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What signals indicate it is safe to move from Partial to Full Automation?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Precision on automated cases is consistently above your acceptable error threshold across multiple weeks. Monitoring dashboards show stable metrics with no degradation. The cost of errors is low enough that automated mistakes are recoverable. You have alerting in place to detect metric drift quickly.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Spectrum Of Automations.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Spectrum Of Automations.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Spectrum Of Automations</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h3>Evaluation</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should I optimize precision first?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Optimize precision first when false positives are expensive, for example blocking legit users or creating costly manual review load.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Evaluation/Classification Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Evaluation/Classification Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classification Evaluation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should I optimize recall first?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Optimize recall first when misses are dangerous, for example fraud, abuse, or safety violations slipping through.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Evaluation/Classification Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Evaluation/Classification Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classification Evaluation</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do I pick a threshold in practice?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Pick a business constraint first, such as recall &gt;= 0.95, then choose the threshold that maximizes the other metric under that constraint. Freeze it and run regression checks on the same golden set.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Evaluation/Classification Evaluation.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Evaluation/Classification Evaluation.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classification Evaluation</a></em></p>
